**Summary of Source 1: Excerpts from "2024LegalStudiesSD\_1.pdf"**

This source provides excerpts from the **VCE Legal Studies Study Design**, version 1.2, updated on 8 January 2024\. It is authorised and published by the Victorian Curriculum and Assessment Authority (VCAA). The publication contains important information about the study, its introduction, scope, rationale, aims, structure, entry requirements, duration, changes, monitoring for quality, safety and wellbeing, employability skills, legislative compliance, and Child Safe Standards. It also details the assessment and reporting procedures for Units 1-4.

The source outlines the **structure of the study**, which is made up of four units: Unit 1: The presumption of innocence, Unit 2: Wrongs and rights, Unit 3: Rights and justice, and Unit 4: The people, the law and reform. Each unit has specific content in areas of study designed to achieve a set of outcomes, described by key knowledge and key skills. Satisfactory completion for a unit requires demonstrating achievement of the set outcomes, determined by assessing a range of learning activities and tasks. Units 3 and 4 must be undertaken as a sequence.

For **Units 3 and 4**, the VCAA specifies assessment procedures for scored assessment. The level of achievement is determined by School-assessed Coursework (SAC) and external assessment. To receive a study score, a student must achieve two or more graded assessments and receive an S for both Units 3 and 4\. The study score is reported on a scale of 0–50. Percentage contributions to the study score are: Unit 3 SAC (25%), Unit 4 SAC (25%), and the end-of-year examination (50%). Authentication rules apply to school-based assessment. The end-of-year examination covers all key knowledge and skills from Units 3 and 4\.

The source provides detailed key knowledge and key skills for each Area of Study (AOS) within Units 1, 2, 3, and 4\.

* **Unit 1: The presumption of innocence** covers legal foundations (sources/types of law, effective laws, parliament-courts relationship, court hierarchy, principles of justice), proving guilt (purposes/concepts of criminal law, types of crime, specific offences, elements, defences, impact), and sanctions (determination of criminal case, enforcement institutions, purposes/types of sanctions, alternative approaches, sentencing practices comparison, principles of justice). Assessment tasks include folios, presentations, reports, essays, structured questions, mock trials, debates, research reports, and media analysis.  
* **Unit 2: Wrongs and rights** covers civil liability (purposes/concepts of civil law, specific areas like negligence/defamation, breach, causation, loss, limitation of actions, burden/standard of proof, parties, elements, defences, remedies, impact), remedies (dispute resolution methods/institutions, purposes/types of remedies, civil cases, principles of justice), and human rights (protection in Australia through Constitution, Charter, statute, common law, possible reforms, contemporary human rights issue with case study). Assessment tasks are similar to Unit 1\.  
* **Unit 3: Rights and justice** examines the Victorian criminal and civil justice systems. Area of Study 1 covers the criminal justice system (summary/indictable offences, key principles like burden/standard of proof, presumption of innocence, rights of accused/victims, principles of justice, VLA/CLCs, plea negotiations, court hierarchy reasons, personnel roles, need for legal practitioners, impact of costs/time/cultural differences, sentencing purposes/types/factors). Area of Study 2 covers the civil justice system (key principles, factors before initiating claim, principles of justice, resolution methods/institutions, court hierarchy reasons, personnel roles, class actions, impact of costs/time, remedies purposes/types). School-assessed Coursework tasks for Unit 3 contribute 25% to the study score.  
* **Unit 4: The people, the law and reform** explores institutions that make and reform laws. Area of Study 1 covers the people and the law-makers (roles of Crown/Parliament in law-making, law-making powers, significance of s109, High Court cases on powers, factors affecting parliament's ability, Constitution as a check \- High Court/representative government, separation of powers, express rights, courts' ability to make law \- statutory interpretation, precedent, factors affecting courts' ability, relationship between courts and parliament). Area of Study 2 covers the people, the law and reform (reasons for law reform, influencing law reform \- individuals/groups/media/law reform bodies, constitutional reform \- reasons, s128 process, referendum factors, 1967/2023 referendums). School-assessed Coursework tasks for Unit 4 contribute 25% to the study score.

The study design incorporates updates, including the **2023 referendum about the Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Voice** into Unit 4, Area of Study 2\.

**Summary of Source 2: Excerpts from "ACFrOgAZ92jZqziJe74ytgEXMLAt59BobajqRGFBYdEGzX5o9LV-cN3vQAAejIkLLeDyl8xBkzya-UFFai1djPL7B6yZsCN4xbVkd09pwq-SfGIHyjoBu14iLpLLiyDOTJbVYNMKowMI-poKmOqUqL987JZiYfsG\_jQXeXvOPw==.pdf"**

This source provides **summary notes for VCE Legal Studies Unit 4, Outcome 1**, covering various legal concepts.

It defines **statutory interpretation** as the process whereby courts give meaning to words in legislation when applying it to a case.

The **doctrine of precedent** is defined as a rule requiring judges to follow the reasons for decisions of superior courts in the same hierarchy for cases with similar facts, based on the principle of 'stare decisis' ('to stand by what has been decided') to ensure consistency, predictability, and justice. The court hierarchy is arranged to uphold this principle.

* **Binding Precedent:** The legal reasoning of a higher court that *must* be followed by all lower courts in the same hierarchy with similar material facts. Following it ensures predictability.  
* **Persuasive Precedent:** Legal reasoning that can act as a guide for judges even though they are not bound to follow it. Judges may use these precedents when deciding a case, especially if there is no applicable law, to help guide their decisions.

The source mentions factors affecting the courts' ability to make law, including **costs**. These can include court filing fees and jury fees. Victoria Legal Aid provides limited financial support, mainly for criminal matters, which may deter potential plaintiffs from initiating civil proceedings due to costs.

The **requirement for standing** (locus standi) is discussed as a factor affecting courts' ability to make law. The party initiating a court claim must show a 'special interest', meaning they are affected by the issue more than the general public. A 'special interest' can include public interest groups, trade unions, businesses, or members of the public directly affected by a government decision.

The **relationship between courts and parliament in law-making** highlights the constant link between these institutions. **Parliamentary supremacy** is the legal concept that parliament can make, amend, or abolish laws, subject to constitutional limitations, and is supreme over other arms of government. As elected representatives, parliament's primary role is to create laws reflecting society's views. Parliament establishes the courts, except for the High Court, and sets out their jurisdictions. However, parliament must adhere to constitutional limitations like the separation of powers at the Commonwealth level, requiring courts to be independent and impartial. Judges may make influential remarks in *obiter dictum* regarding the current state of the law.

**Summary of Source 3: Excerpts from "ACFrOgB5vzIrb3KErFDtX3bzK9WaGW3PC96NN1WSP4ZmTe-XQMD\_J7WoQ8vfVkSC7jA3Nv9Akblb0Q5tlqkKx5T4SMTnR0aQs\_CnTOy4KAeegh\_BxZXkG6PQlrCG0pLbWSH\_W4klkGn8\_32bi1x8FCV7NiHmJOv9lO\_hjkRSg==.pdf"**

This source provides **summary notes for VCE Legal Studies Unit 4, Outcome 1**, focusing on the people and law-makers.

It outlines the **roles of the Commonwealth Parliament**:

* **House of Representatives:** The lower house with 151 members representing electorates.  
* **Senate:** The upper house with 76 elected members, often called the 'states' house' or 'house of review'.  
* **The Crown:** Represented by the Governor-General, whose primary role in law-making is to grant royal assent for a bill to become law.

It also outlines the **roles of the Victorian Parliament**:

* **Legislative Assembly:** The lower house with 88 members representing electoral districts.  
* **Legislative Council:** The upper house with 40 elected members, often referred to as the 'house of review'.  
* **The Crown:** Represented by the Governor, whose primary role is to grant royal assent for a bill to become state legislation.

The **division of powers** refers to the constitutional division of law-making powers between the Commonwealth and state parliaments to avoid abuses of power and share responsibility.

* **Residual Powers:** Belong solely to state parliaments and are not stated in the Constitution, such as education, agriculture, and police.  
* **Concurrent Powers:** Law-making powers granted to *both* Commonwealth and state parliaments, listed in s51 of the Constitution, such as taxation, marriage and divorce, and banking.  
* **Exclusive Powers:** Law-making powers granted *only* to the Commonwealth Parliament by s51 and s52 of the Constitution, such as currency, overseas trade, and foreign affairs.

**Section 109 of the Australian Constitution** addresses conflicts where Commonwealth and state laws are inconsistent. It states that the Commonwealth law shall prevail to the extent of the inconsistency, and the state law shall be invalid. Its significance is that it can act as a restriction on state parliaments in areas of concurrent power.

The **bicameral structure** (a law-making body with two houses) can affect law-making. A 'hostile upper house' occurs when the government lacks a majority in the upper house, giving the balance of power to other parties. If the majority in both houses shares political views, bills may pass easily, potentially limiting the upper house's review role.

The **representative nature of parliament** (representative government) is established and protected by sections 7 and 24 of the Constitution, requiring members of the Senate and House of Representatives to be 'directly chosen by the people' to represent their views. The High Court's interpretation of these sections has protected this principle, for example, by recognising the implied right to freedom of political communication.

The **separation of powers** is a constitutional principle ensuring the legislative, executive, and judicial powers remain separate to prevent abuse of power.

* **Legislative Power:** Vested in parliament to make laws.  
* **Executive Power:** Applied by the Crown/Governor-General and in practice the Cabinet, to administer laws.  
* **Judicial Power:** Vested in courts and tribunals to enforce laws and resolve legal matters. This separation acts as a check on parliament.

The Constitution also acts as a check on parliament through the **express protection of rights**. Sections of the Constitution explicitly state rights, such as the acquisition of property on just terms (s51(xxxi)), trial by jury for Commonwealth offences (s80), interstate trade and commerce (s92), freedom of religion (s116), and no discrimination based on state of residence (s117).

The source concludes with examples of **exam practice questions** covering topics like international treaties and external affairs, the separation of powers as a check, the Australian people as a check, and the High Court's impact on the division of powers.

**Summary of Source 4: Excerpts from "APznzaYAARdoHX269BNAQy4OFzw0nAyIzckgEk37YziBfli800b\_7-b4nxdaBF9zYIXSX0rhb4xCC\_x8Rmw0ySidziCwm9RJ3UZpRBcHjn5qEUUAifZiAyrIrQsw9fpYVis5rnNH\_Og-bStpSBn4ojvv5BsOv01JMFyhaZSa04S1Ww8b98gBX0RO6QdM3L6FEfBJYkF0-nO8eFalfyNSpXYwtIi5Y5bGRh5Y3uk5T30oXi0.pdf"**

This source provides content related to **Law-making powers**, drawing on material suitable for VCE Legal Studies.

It introduces the **Australian Constitution** as a set of rules establishing the nature, functions, and limits of government, which came into force on 1 January 1901\. Key features include establishing the Commonwealth Parliament, the High Court, matters relating to states, law-making powers, and a mechanism for change (referendum).

The **Commonwealth Parliament** consists of the King (represented by the Governor-General), the Senate (upper house), and the House of Representatives (lower house).

* **House of Representatives:** The lower house ('people's house') with 151 members, where the party with the majority forms the government. Its roles include initiating and making laws, determining government, representing people, scrutinising government administration, acting as a house of review, and controlling government expenditure (initiates money bills).  
* **Senate:** The upper house ('states' house' or 'house of review') with 76 members (12 per state, 2 per territory), elected by proportional representation. Its main role is to review laws from the House of Representatives, and its roles also include allowing equal state representation, scrutinising bills and government administration, and initiating and passing bills.

The **Victorian Parliament** consists of the King (represented by the Governor), the Legislative Council (upper house), and the Legislative Assembly (lower house).

* **Legislative Assembly:** The lower house with 88 members, where the majority party forms government. Its roles include initiating and passing bills, forming government, representing the people, acting as a house of review, controlling government expenditure, and scrutinising government administration.  
* **Legislative Council:** The upper house with 40 members from eight regions. Its primary role is to act as a house of review, scrutinising government administration, and initiating and passing bills.

The **Crown** (British monarch) is part of the system via the Governor-General (federal) and Governors (state). The three main roles in law-making are granting royal assent (approving bills), withholding royal assent (power to refuse), and appointing the Executive Council (advises Crown and approves secondary legislation). Royal assent is given on the advice of the Prime Minister/Premier or ministers.

The **division of law-making powers** under the Australian Constitution includes Residual, Exclusive, and Concurrent powers.

* **Residual Powers:** Powers left with the states, not listed in the Constitution, such as criminal law, education, and public transport.  
* **Exclusive Powers:** Powers only the Commonwealth Parliament can exercise, specifically mentioned in s51 and 52, or exclusive by nature (e.g., naturalisation), such as defence, currency, and customs.  
* **Concurrent Powers:** Powers shared by both Commonwealth and state parliaments, listed in s51, such as trade, taxation, and marriage.

**Section 109 of the Constitution** resolves conflicts between state and Commonwealth laws. If inconsistent, the Commonwealth law prevails to the extent of the inconsistency, making the state provisions invalid. This acts as a restriction on state parliaments. The *McBain v Victoria (2000)* case is given as an example of inconsistency in marital status law regarding infertility treatment.

The **High Court** has jurisdiction to hear cases involving the Commonwealth or disputes between states. Its decisions in cases involving law-making power disputes have affected the division of powers.

* **The Brislan case (1935):** Involved interpreting s51(v) ('postal, telegraphic, telephonic and other like services'). The High Court held that the *Wireless Telegraphy Act 1905 (Cth)*, requiring licences for wireless sets, was valid, interpreting 'other like services' to include broadcasting to a wireless set. Significance: Shifted law-making power from states to Commonwealth, making wireless sets an area of concurrent power, and influenced interpretation for future technologies like television and potentially the internet.  
* **The Tasmanian Dams case (1983):** Involved interpreting s51(xxix) ('external affairs'). The Commonwealth passed the *World Heritage Properties Conservation Act 1983 (Cth)* to prohibit the Tasmanian dam construction, relying on its obligations under the UNESCO World Heritage Convention. The High Court held the external affairs power allows the Commonwealth to make laws to fulfil international treaty obligations, validating the Commonwealth Act and making the inconsistent Tasmanian law invalid under s109. Significance: Shifted law-making power to the Commonwealth in areas previously considered residual (state), allowing the Commonwealth to move into new areas based on international treaties, although there are limitations (treaty must be genuine, law cannot expand beyond treaty or infringe express rights, state power isn't entirely removed).

**Summary of Source 5: Excerpts from "APznzaYVIpAOp3xI8SbHfnq3N9TMuUhZuBbIGWfjqEvs89yczOVUwbwctA89ke1n90zpta2D8bsY7gAvsONO2M-RWL3ek6hqdOA1kZd8iNICALfmv1fy2pjZzoDpRuXi6LfqVcRulVrKFSmS7o9hmDb3F8AnU88yddd73IDtneWTayiTXZ2F3VXtRqLHr58O11biNyYvGzxo6-lBlh1BQSWiqprqkUgyBihhdiD2AW\_jVCNO.pdf"**

This source presents a diagram illustrating the **policy development and parliamentary processes for creating a bill in the Victorian Parliament**.

Ideas for policy can originate from the public service, government/opposition, media, public opinion, ministerial advisers, and political parties. Policy development may involve reading submissions and gaining Cabinet approval.

The **preparation of a bill** involves Cabinet approval (in principle and final), review by a Minister's Bill Committee, administrative amendments by the public service, and drafting by Parliamentary Counsel. Political parties also discuss party policy.

Bills, including Private Member's Bills, may originate in either the Legislative Assembly or Legislative Council, except for Bills to spend government money or raise taxes, which must start in the Legislative Assembly.

The parliamentary processes in the **House of Origin** (either Legislative Assembly or Legislative Council) typically involve several stages:

1. **Initiation:** The Minister or Member introduces the bill.  
2. **First Reading:** A formal motion to allow the bill to proceed.  
3. **Second Reading:** The Minister/Member tables a statement of compatibility with the *Charter of Human Rights and Responsibilities Act 2006 (Vic)*. A speech explains the bill's purpose, and the bill is made public, followed by debate on its general principles.  
4. **Consideration in Detail:** (Optional stage) The bill is examined clause by clause, and amendments can be made. In the Legislative Council, this is called the 'Committee of the Whole' stage.  
5. **Third Reading:** Further debate if necessary, followed by a final vote on the bill.

If the bill passes the House of Origin, it proceeds to the other House.

**Summary of Source 6: Excerpts from "APznzaZyLSR6SmD6Bn0MISBpKm8RRQPuqD6Tzy6kbZmxA5cKylfGkYUAeUeoH2TF0ODIt0NJXmQPteQH-mWtqOIjf7BCx4pYqDbQ14jzKV1ZuJSZ1XJAyVYuC-2O682dTr-3doVjHGx\_xFvKQwH6YQ85Xc601v-pgYI1l94abiUErGeL5dSOHgPK6I3RP9Silbvg2iGyzPoND4W-Ff2SJo8Mq18LN9aAD1Y3XA2EHgvT85nCE.pdf"**

This source presents a news report titled "**Pressure to legislate**", discussing aspects of the Commonwealth Parliament.

It notes the significant **pressures on modern parliaments** regarding the volume of legislation. The average number of hours Parliament has sat per year has increased significantly since 1901\. Legislation passes more quickly through the House of Representatives (lower house) than the Senate (upper house). The Senate is seen as a check on government legislation, especially when the government lacks a majority there. However, debate on public matters often occurs in the lower house.

The **crucial roles of the Senate** are highlighted. The Senate was primarily created to protect the interests of the states. It acts as a check on government legislation by scrutinising bills, delegated legislation, and government administration, often through committee procedures. The Senate can also initiate, amend, or reject bills. The committee system allows scrutiny of bills, assessing their impact on individual rights and the rule of law.

The source also evaluates the **strengths and weaknesses of the Senate**.

* **Strengths:** The Senate's number of members. It acts as a house of review. It ensures the doctrine of precedent is upheld. It promotes equal representation of states. It scrutinises bills and government administration. Crossbench support can allow for improved bills. Proportional representation provides broader representation. The committee process allows for detailed scrutiny. It allows citizens to express views via petitions/submissions. The system is debated and developed.  
* **Weaknesses:** Senators may vote according to party policies, not state interests. Equal representation may favour smaller states. Crossbench support may be crucial. Lack of a government majority can slow law-making. It may not fully represent electorates' diverse interests. Senators may not be responsive to the electorate.

**Summary of Source 7: Excerpts from "APznzaaSE4Yye5mGb855SzGWVteu2\_lQGdXl5Iy3DPtLBPGcaFRl2uM0z-L65w-HX41-UyB0N\_Y0xuFMg5DGLD8lq-jd8v80Y6TFTj0eScoLjCofhqNSkAvR-eCdS9f3fFXVc1uy6Q7PPUvdz9XsjkSUs2m-3aKGuZHtAgnmoQihNULVIyURu9zQ1w3qm3HP-RJ65Sp0t9-RNWHi6ndjqPYNJdVdDwBTzqftwDtZ-l.pdf"**

This source provides material related to **VCE Legal Studies Unit 4: The people, the law and reform, specifically Outcome 1**, covering the ability of parliament and courts to make law and constitutional checks on parliament. It lists the key knowledge and key skills for this outcome.

It focuses on **Parliament and the Australian Constitution**, with an aim to understand the role of the Commonwealth and Victorian Parliaments in law-making. It includes review questions on prior knowledge about the Australian legal system.

The **structure of the Victorian Parliament** and the **Commonwealth Parliament** is presented.

* **Commonwealth Parliament:** Consists of the King's Representative (Governor-General), Lower House (House of Representatives), and Upper House (Senate). Resources from APH.GOV.AU are mentioned.  
  * **House of Representatives:** Main roles are to represent the people, introduce and pass bills, review Senate bills, and form government. It has 151 members representing electorates. Roles in law-making include initiating/making laws, determining government, providing responsible government, representing the people, publicising/scrutinising government administration, controlling government expenditure (initiates money bills), and acting as a house of review for Senate bills.  
  * **Senate:** Main roles are to represent the interests of states/territories, introduce/pass bills, and review House of Representatives bills. Often called the house of review or states' house. Provides for representative government by safeguarding state interests (s7 of the Constitution). Provides for responsible government by influencing and holding the government accountable. Scrutinises government through committees. Initiates and passes bills (not money bills). The source includes questions based on a 'Pressure to Legislate' article (Source 6).  
* **Victorian Parliament:** Consists of the King (represented by the Governor), Legislative Council (upper house), and Legislative Assembly (lower house). Resources from PARLIAMENT.VIC.GOV.AU are mentioned.  
  * **Legislative Assembly:** The lower house with 88 members representing electoral districts. Main function is to make laws, sharing this role with the Legislative Council. Key functions include determining government, providing representative government, providing responsible government, scrutinising government, and controlling government spending.  
  * **Legislative Council:** Primary role is to act as a house of review. Key functions include reviewing laws from the Legislative Assembly, providing for representative government (representing people in regions), and scrutinising government.

**How government is formed** is explained. Parliament includes all members of both houses and the Crown. Government refers to the political party with the majority of seats in the Lower House. The leader becomes the Prime Minister or Premier. The next largest party forms the opposition.

The source includes multiple choice questions and 'Check your learning' sections throughout.

**Summary of Source 9: Excerpts from "How a law is made \- Parliament of Victoria"**

This source, from the **Parliament of Victoria website**, explains the **process of passing a law**. Law-making is one of Parliament's key roles. The process involves six steps.

1. **An idea:** Ideas can come from anyone, including members of Parliament, political parties, the public, lobby groups, parliamentary committees, the media, and the public service/ministerial advisers.  
2. **Creating a bill:** The idea is drafted into a document called a bill by the Office of the Chief Parliamentary Counsel. It can be a government bill (from a minister) or a private member's bill (from a non-minister member).  
3. **Legislative Assembly:** Most bills start here and go through several stages ('readings').  
   * **Introduction and first reading:** Bill is introduced, members vote to consider it, and a statement of compatibility with the *Victorian Charter of Human Rights and Responsibilities 2006* is tabled.  
   * **Second reading:** Minister/member explains purpose, bill is made public, debate is adjourned for review, then members debate and vote.  
   * **Consideration in detail:** (Optional) Bill is examined clause by clause, questions asked, amendments voted on.  
   * **Third reading:** Final vote to approve the bill.  
4. **Legislative Council:** Once approved by the Assembly, the bill is sent to the Legislative Council. It goes through the same stages: Introduction and first reading, Second reading, Committee of the whole (equivalent to Consideration in detail), and Third reading. If the Council amends the bill, it returns to the Assembly. Some bills can start in the Council, but both houses must complete all stages, except for the yearly budget, which only requires Assembly approval.  
5. **Royal assent:** The approved bill is presented to the Governor of Victoria for royal assent. Once received, it becomes an Act of Parliament (a law of Victoria). The Governor cannot refuse assent for political or personal reasons.  
6. **A new law:** The law commences either immediately upon royal assent, on a date specified in the law, or a date decided later by the Governor.

The source also provides links to track legislation progress.

---

**Summary of Source 10: Excerpts from "LEGAL34\_Chapter10\_Revision\_notes.pdf"**

This source provides **revision notes for VCE Legal Studies Chapter 10: Law-making powers**. It serves as a foundation for students' notes.

It introduces the **Australian Constitution** as a set of rules and principles establishing the nature, functions, powers, duties, and limits of government. It is a formal written constitution. Features include establishing the Commonwealth Parliament, the High Court, matters relating to states, law-making powers of the Commonwealth, and the referendum mechanism for change.

The **Commonwealth Parliament** consists of the King (represented by the Governor-General), the Senate (upper house), and the House of Representatives (lower house).

* **House of Representatives:** Lower house ('people's house') with 151 members. The majority party forms the government, led by the Prime Minister. Roles in law-making: initiate/make laws, determine government, act as house of review, control government expenditure, represent people, scrutinise government administration.  
* **Senate:** Upper house ('states' house' or 'house of review') with 76 members (12 per state, 2 per territory).  
* **Victorian Parliament:** Consists of the King (represented by the Governor), Legislative Council (upper house), and Legislative Assembly (lower house).  
  * **Legislative Assembly:** Lower house of the Victorian Parliament with 88 members. Majority party forms government, led by the Premier.  
  * **Legislative Council:** Upper house of the Victorian Parliament.  
* **The Crown:** Represented by one Governor-General (federal) and six governors (state). Main responsibility is to ensure the democratic system operates effectively. Roles in law-making: granting royal assent (formal signing/approval), withholding royal assent (rare), appointing Executive Council (advises Crown's representative).

The **division of law-making powers** are powers given to parliament in certain areas. The Australian Constitution divides these into:

* **Residual powers:** Left with the states, Commonwealth has no authority in these areas, not listed in Constitution (e.g., criminal law, education, public transport).  
* **Exclusive powers:** Held only by the Commonwealth Parliament, listed in s51 and s52, or exclusive by nature (e.g., naturalisation), or made exclusive by other sections (e.g., s114 naval forces) (e.g., defence, currency, customs).  
* **Concurrent powers:** Shared by both Commonwealth and state parliaments, listed in s51 (e.g., trade, taxation, marriage).

**Section 109** resolves inconsistencies between state and Commonwealth laws. If inconsistent, the Commonwealth law prevails to the extent of the inconsistency, making the state law invalid and unenforceable. Significance: acts as a restriction on state parliaments in concurrent areas, but does not automatically operate and must be challenged in court.

The **High Court and the division of law-making powers**. The High Court has jurisdiction under s75 to hear disputes involving the Commonwealth or between states. High Court cases have affected the division of powers.

* **The Brislan case (1935):** Interpreted s51(v) ('postal, telegraphic, telephonic and other like services'). Involved the *Wireless Telegraphy Act 1905 (Cth)*. Significance: Extended Commonwealth power to broadcasting to a wireless set, shifting power from states to Commonwealth (making it concurrent). Influenced interpretation for television (*Jones v Commonwealth*) and potentially the internet.  
* **The Tasmanian Dam case (1983):** Interpreted s51(xxix) ('external affairs'). Involved the *Gordon River Hydro-Electric Power Development Act 1982 (Tas)* and the Commonwealth's *World Heritage Properties Conservation Act 1983 (Cth)* based on the World Heritage Convention treaty. High Court held the external affairs power allows the Commonwealth to create laws to fulfil international treaty obligations. Significance: Commonwealth could move into a residual (state) area, shifting power to Commonwealth. Limitations exist (treaty must be genuine, law cannot expand beyond treaty or infringe express rights, state power remains).

**Summary of Source 11: Excerpts from "UNIT 4 – AOS 1 & 2 LEGAL STUDIES SUMMARY NOTES FOR THE VCAA EXAMS WRITTEN BY A STUDENT WHO OBTAINED A NEAR PERFECT STUDY S \- TSFX"**

This source provides **summary notes for VCE Legal Studies Unit 4, Areas of Study 1 & 2**, written for VCAA exams.

It covers the **roles of the Crown and Houses of Parliament** in law-making.

* **Crown:** Provides royal assent, acts as head of executive government (appointing judges, setting sitting times), and exercises reserve powers (dissolve Parliament, withhold assent, appoint ministers).  
* **Lower House:** Initiates most new bills, forms the government, acts as the 'people's house' reflecting constituent views. Private member bills are unlikely to pass but raise awareness. Majority government means easier bill passage. Hung parliament requires reliance on minor parties/independents, watering down bills.  
* **Upper House:** Acts as a house of review, scrutinising and amending bills from the lower house, often not controlled by the government. Represents regional interests and allows smaller states protection. Elected using proportional voting. A hostile upper house can reject many bills.

The **division of constitutional law-making powers** includes Specific powers (umbrella term for exclusive/concurrent), Exclusive powers (solely Commonwealth, e.g., customs, defence, currency), Concurrent powers (shared by Commonwealth and states, e.g., marriage, trade, taxation), and Residual powers (solely states, not in Constitution, e.g., education, public transport).

The **significance of s109** ('inconsistency rule') is its application when Commonwealth and state laws conflict in concurrent areas. The Commonwealth law prevails, and the inconsistent state sections are invalid. It constrains state powers but requires a High Court challenge to be declared invalid. The *McBain* case is cited as an example where s109 was applied, shifting discrimination law to be concurrent.

The **means by which the Constitution acts as a check on parliament** are detailed:

* **Bicameral Structure:** Upper house scrutinises legislation. Strengths: Scrutiny limits abuse, hostile upper house provides consideration. Weaknesses: Rubber-stamping Senate if government holds majority, hostile Senate can waste sitting days on minority needs.  
* **Separation of Powers:** Legislative, executive, judicial powers are separate (constitutionally at federal level) to prevent abuse. Executive powers (s61) are exercised by Governor-General and Cabinet. Legislative powers (s1) vested in Parliament. Judicial powers (s71) vested in High Court and federal courts. Strengths: Legislature scrutinises executive (e.g., question time), independent judiciary checks parliamentary powers (s76). Weaknesses: Overlap of legislative/executive, rubber-stamping/antagonistic Senate compromises scrutiny, courts cannot second guess efficacy, less binding at state level.  
* **Express Protection of Rights:** Rights explicitly stated and entrenched in the Constitution, changeable only by referendum (s128). Five rights listed (trial by jury s80, freedom of religion s116, etc., citing *Brown v The Queen*). Strengths: Entrenched, restrict parliament's law-making, enforceable by High Court. Weaknesses: Referendum is difficult, court challenges are costly/time consuming, rights are limited in scope and application (mostly federal).  
* **Role of the High Court in Interpreting the Constitution:** High Court established (s71) with power under s76 to hear constitutional disputes. Roles include interpreting the Constitution and invalidating unconstitutional laws. Strengths: Independent of politics, interpretation allows flexibility (implied rights like freedom of political communication from *ACTV* case), interpretations are binding. Weaknesses: Can only rule on cases brought before it (retrospective), limited role due to litigation cost/time, cannot change wording.  
* **Requirement for a Double Majority in a Referendum:** Constitution wording changed only via referendum (s128) requiring approval of Commonwealth Houses and a double majority (majority of voters nationwide and majority in majority of states). Only 8/44 succeeded. Strengths: Obstruction on Parliament's arbitrary power grabs, protects smaller states, compulsory vote empowers people. Weaknesses: Public may lack understanding, double majority is difficult, timely/costly process, result may seem unjust if national majority but not state majority.

The **significance of specific cases/referendums** is covered:

* *Roach v Electoral Commissioner (2007)*: Interpreted s7 and s24, affirming structural right to vote for most adults, restricting Commonwealth's ability to legislate this away without good reason.  
* 1967 Referendum: Amended s51(xxvi) and removed s127, giving Commonwealth power to legislate for Indigenous people and including them in population counts. Significance: Shifted power regarding Indigenous affairs to concurrent.  
* *Commonwealth v Tasmania (1983)* (Tasmanian Dam case): Interpreted s51(xxix) ('external affairs') to include implementing international treaty obligations. Significance: Shifted power to Commonwealth in residual areas covered by treaties (making them concurrent). Scope affected by international instruments.  
* *R v Brislan (1935)*: Interpreted s51(v) ('other like services') to include wireless sets. Significance: Shifted power to Commonwealth regarding communication, making it concurrent (including television, potentially internet).

**Introduction to Parliament** covers what parliament is (assembly of elected representatives to make laws), roles of houses, and representative government (people choose MPs to make laws on their behalf, established by s7 and s24).

Factors affecting Parliament's ability to make law include the role of the houses, representative nature, political pressures (from groups, international forces like UN or Amnesty International), and restrictions on powers (specific prohibitions like s116 religion ban).

The **doctrine of precedent** (sare decisis, ratio decidendi, binding/persuasive, obiter dicta) is explained. Ways of developing/avoiding precedent (reversing, overruling, distinguishing \- *Davies v Waldron*, disapproving \- *Trigwell*) and how common law expands are covered. Strengths and disadvantages of courts in law-making via precedent are listed.

**Statutory interpretation** (courts giving meaning to legislation) is defined. Reasons for interpretation include issues during drafting, applying legislation (general terms, changing nature of words, ambiguous meaning \- *Deing v Tarola*, *Davies v Waldron*, *Kevin and Jennifer*), and unforeseen circumstances. Effects include creating precedent, broadening/narrowing statutes, and prompting legislative change. Statutory interpretation can impact Parliament by clarifying statutes and shifting power.

Factors affecting courts' ability to make law include the doctrine of precedent, judicial conservatism (reluctance to develop new law) and judicial activism, costs and time (litigation can be costly/time-consuming but urgent matters can be quick), and the requirement for standing (direct effect, special interest). Secondary (delegated) legislation is also mentioned.

The relationship between courts and parliament includes the **supremacy of parliament** (can abrogate common law, except constitutional matters), **ability of courts to influence parliament** (obiter dictum, judicial decisions prompt legislative action), **codification of common law** (parliament affirms court decisions, e.g., *Mabo* case and *Native Title Act 1993 (Cth)*), and **abrogation of common law** (parliament overrides court decisions, unless constitutional).

**Reasons for law reform** include characteristics of an effective law (known, understood, changeable, enforceable, acceptable).

**Means by which individuals or groups can influence law reform** include petitions (written requests, paper/e-petitions, strengths/weaknesses), demonstrations (public gatherings, strengths/weaknesses), and the use of courts (challenging laws, establishing precedent).

The **role of the media** (social media, traditional media) in law reform involves generating community interest and attracting attention of MPs. Strengths and weaknesses of media influence are discussed (fostering attention, direct engagement, investigations vs simplification, inaccuracy, bias).

**Law reform bodies** covered:

* **Victorian Law Reform Commission (VLRC):** Independent body reviewing/researching Victorian laws and making recommendations. Roles: Inquiry (on Attorney-General reference), Investigation (minor issues), Monitor, Education. Process: reference/proposal, research/consult, expert panel, consultation paper, submissions, consultations, report, delivery to AG, tabling in parliament, government decision, parliament legislation. Example: medicinal cannabis report (recommendations adopted). Strengths: VLRC expertise/resources, recommendations often adopted, community/expert input, reforms whole areas, independent. Weaknesses: No obligation to adopt recommendations, limited influence if no bipartisan support, restricted by terms of reference.  
* **Royal Commissions:** Highest form of public inquiry into matters of public concern. Established by the Crown's representative (executive) at Commonwealth/state level. Process: letters patent issued, issue paper, consultation, public hearings (compel evidence), report with recommendations. Example: Royal Commission into Family Violence (recommendations adopted). Strengths: Deferral of investigation to commission, recommendations likely acted on, gauge opinion, compel evidence, independent, reform whole areas. Weaknesses: Can be used as political tool, no obligation to adopt recommendations, costly, limited influence without bipartisan support, restricted by terms of reference.

The **ability of parliament and courts to respond to law reform** is evaluated. Strengths of parliament: Elected (representative), supreme law-maker (codify/abrogate common law), directs investigatory bodies (VLRC, Royal Commissions), responds in futuro (prospectively), delegates powers (secondary legislation).

**Summary of Source 12: Excerpts from "Unit 4 AOS 1 \- THE PEOPLE AND THE AUSTRALIAN CONSTITUTION | VCE Legal Studies"**

This source provides notes for **VCE Legal Studies Unit 4 Area of Study 1: The People and the Australian Constitution**.

It outlines the **roles of the Crown and Houses of Parliament** in law-making.

* **Commonwealth:** Governor-General (Crown), House of Reps, Senate. House of Reps is the 'people's house'/'house of government', initiates most new laws, determines government, provides responsible/representative government, scrutinises government, controls expenditure (initiates money bills), acts as review house for Senate bills. Senate is the 'states' house'/'house of review', scrutinises bills (via committees), can initiate bills (not money bills), pass/amend/reject bills. Crown's rep (Governor-General) grants royal assent, can withhold (rare), appoints Executive Council (advises, approves secondary legislation).  
* **Victorian:** Governor (Crown), Legislative Assembly, Legislative Council. Legislative Assembly initiates/passes bills, forms government, provides representative government, acts as review house for Legislative Council bills, controls government expenditure. Legislative Council is primary house of review, scrutinises, debates, amends/rejects legislation, examines bills via committees, initiates (less common) and passes bills. Crown's rep (Governor) grants royal assent, can withhold (rare), acts as Head of State, chairs Executive Council.

The **division of constitutional law-making powers**.

* **Residual powers:** Not given to Commonwealth, thus states (crim law, public transport, education), protected by s106, 107, 108\.  
* **Exclusive powers:** Solely Commonwealth (defence, currency, immigration, naturalisation), specified in Const (s51-2+).  
* **Concurrent powers:** Exercisable by both Commonwealth and states (trade, taxation, marriage/divorce, communication), protected by s51, can lead to conflicting laws.

The **significance of Section 109**. It resolves conflicting laws from concurrent powers. If inconsistent, Commonwealth law prevails, state law invalid to extent of inconsistency. Significance: restricts state parliaments in concurrent areas where Commonwealth law exists, imposes consistency (Commonwealth prevails). Limitation: does not automatically operate, requires court challenge.

The **means by which the Australian Constitution acts as a check on parliament** are discussed.

* **Bicameral structure:** S1 states two houses for CommP. Senate acts as house of review. Strengths: upper house scrutiny checks abuse, debate can occur if slim majority/hung parliament, hostile Senate increases scrutiny. Weaknesses: rubber stamping if government has majority in both houses, no Const requirement for states to be bicameral, laws supporting by people may be dismissed if not aligned with Fed Gov policies.  
* **Separation of legislative, executive and judicial powers:** Doctrine ensures powers are separate (federal level) to prevent abuse. Executive power (s61) administered by Gov-Gen/Cabinet. Legislative power (s1) by Parliament. Judicial power (s71) by HCA/fed courts. Strengths: executive scrutiny by legislature, judiciary independence checks parliamentary powers (ultra vires), abolition requires referendum. Weaknesses: overlap of legis/exec limits check, rubber stamping limits scrutiny if gov controls Senate, judicial appointments by exec may show influence, only applies at fed level. Explains separation acts as a check guarding against abuse of power, but overlap between legis/exec may compromise this.  
* **Express protection of rights:** Rights enumerated and entrenched in Constitution, changeable only by referendum (s128). Examples: s51(xxxi) property on just terms, s80 trial by jury (limited), s92 interstate trade, s116 religion, s117 no state discrimination. Strengths: limit law-making areas, judicial check (HCA can invalidate infringements), entrenched, stability allows public awareness. Weaknesses: referendum is difficult, challenging costs money, limited scope (mostly Comm parl), limited number of rights. Explains express rights act as a check by prohibiting certain laws and being entrenched, but limited scope and cost of challenging may reduce effectiveness.  
* **Role of the HCA in interpreting the constitution:** HCA established (s71), s76 gives jurisdiction for Const disputes. Acts as 'guardian' interpreting wording (not changing), can invalidate unconstitutional laws (ultra vires). Strengths: judicial independence, allows challenges/laws overturned, expertise ensures proper decisions, independent check on abuse. Weaknesses: rules only on facts of case (not general principles), limited role due to litigation expense, interprets not changes wording. Explains HCA acts as check guarding against abuse by invalidating ultra vires laws, but limited by need for a case with standing (costly/difficult).  
* **Requirement for a double majority in a referendum:** Const wording changed only by referendum (s128). Requires approval of CommP and double majority (national majority and majority in majority of states). Strengths: empowers voters to vote against inappropriate changes, strict double majority filters changes, protects smaller states, compulsory voting empowers people. Weaknesses: laypeople may not understand complex details, double majority is difficult, timely/costly, result may seem unjust if national majority but not state majority. Explains referendum acts as check ensuring Const changes have public approval, limiting CommP power.

The **significance of specific High Court cases** is detailed:

* **Interpreting sections 7 and 24:** *Roach v Electoral Commissioner (2007)*. Facts: Prisoner challenged law banning all prisoners from voting as inconsistent with representative government (s7/24). Decision: Amendment invalid as inconsistent with representative government principle, but original ban (3+ yrs) valid. Significance: Affirmed structural right to vote for adults, restricting CommP from legislating this away without good reason.  
* **Impacting the division of constitutional law-making powers:** *R v Brislan (1935)*. Facts: Challenge to *Wireless Telegraphy Act (Cth)* based on s51(v) ('other like services'). Decision: 'Like services' included wireless sets, Act valid. Significance: Shifted communication power to concurrent, broadened CommP legislative ability (includes television, internet).

The **impact of international declarations and treaties on the interpretation of the external affairs power** (s51(xxix)) is explained. Treaties are binding agreements ratified by Parliament, declarations are non-binding statements.

* *Tasmanian Dams case (Commonwealth v Tasmania (1983))*. Facts: Tasmanian dam construction under state residual power vs CommP law based on UNESCO World Heritage treaty obligation. Decision: External affairs power includes fulfilling international treaty obligations. Impact: Shifted power to Commonwealth to legislate in residual areas covered by treaties (making them concurrent), increased CommP ability.

A template for discussing High Court cases is provided.
