**Summary of Source 16: Excerpts from "legalstudies unit4aos1.pdf"**

This source provides detailed **notes for VCE Legal Studies Unit 4, Area of Study 1: The people and the law-makers**. It covers how the Australian Constitution establishes law-making powers and acts as a check on parliament, the significance of the High Court, parliament and courts in law-making, and their relationship. It lists the key skills for Outcome 1\.

**Chapter 7: Parliament and the Australian Constitution** is outlined.

* **Lesson 7A: The Commonwealth Parliament and the Crown in law-making:** Describes Australia as a constitutional monarchy with a federal, bicameral parliament. Details the roles of the House of Representatives (lower house, 'house of the people', initiates most bills, forms government, represents people) and the Senate (upper house, 'states' house'/'house of review', scrutinises legislation, acts as states' house, can initiate legislation). Explains the role of the Governor-General (Crown's representative) in granting/withholding royal assent and summoning the Executive Council.  
* **Lesson 7B: The Victorian Parliament and the Crown in law-making:** Describes the Victorian Parliament's structure (Legislative Assembly/Council, Governor). Details the roles of the Legislative Assembly (lower house, initiates most legislation, initiates financial bills, represents people, reviews/amends legislation) and the Legislative Council (upper house, 'house of review', scrutinises legislation, can initiate legislation). Explains the role of the Governor (Crown's representative) in granting/withholding royal assent, acting as Head of State, and chairing the Executive Council. Provides a comparison of Victorian and Commonwealth Parliament roles.  
* **Lesson 7C: The division of powers:** Explains the split of law-making powers (exclusive, concurrent, residual) between Commonwealth and state parliaments to avoid abuse and share responsibility. Defines and gives examples of Exclusive powers (Commonwealth only), Concurrent powers (shared), and Residual powers (states only). Mentions s51 and s52 for exclusive/concurrent and that residual are not in the Constitution.  
* **Lesson 7D: Section 109 of the Australian Constitution:** Explains s109 resolves inconsistencies between state and Commonwealth laws in concurrent areas (Commonwealth law prevails to extent of inconsistency). Significance: resolves inconsistencies, restricts state law-making in concurrent areas, requires court challenge for state law to be invalid, allows state laws to revive if Commonwealth law changes. Mentions *McBain v Victoria* as an example.  
* **Lesson 7E: High Court cases and their impact on law-making powers:** High Court interprets the Constitution and can Brislan (1935)\*: Interpreted s51(v) 'other like services' to include radio broadcasting. Significance: Confirmed/broadened Commonwealth power over broadcasting and new communication technologies like the internet. Limitations: Future challenges possible, expansion relies on future cases. Also mentions *Jones v The Commonwealth* (television) and the NBN (*Electronic Transactions Act 1999 (Cth)*) as further examples of relying on this interpretation. Parliament cannot overrule HCA decisions.  
* **Lesson 7F: Parliament’s ability to make law – the bicameral structure:** Explains the bicameral structure (two houses) in Commonwealth and Victorian Parliaments (s1 of Const, s15 of *Constitution Act 1975 (Vic)*). Describes the law-making process through both houses (readings, royal assent). Factors affecting ability include composition of upper house: hostile upper house (government lacks majority, increases scrutiny but can slow/skew process) and rubber stamp (government has majority, efficient but less scrutiny). Strengths: second house review checks government, adequate debate/consideration. Limitations: rubber stamping bypasses scrutiny, hostile house can slow process, limited sitting days limit legislative activity, specific interests in hostile house may be prioritised.  
* **Lesson 7G: Parliament’s ability to make law – international pressures:** Explains international pressures (influence from other countries/organisations) to comply with international law (treaties/declarations). International treaties are binding upon ratification, implemented via domestic legislation (s51(xxix) external affairs power). Failure to comply can lead to criticism. International declarations are non-binding but influential (e.g., UDHR). United Nations (UN) (founding Member State, UN Charter treaty) pressures via Universal Periodic Review (UPR) and recommendations (non-binding but influential). Other international organisations (G20, WTO \- can impose sanctions, Amnesty International, Greenpeace) also exert pressure. Strengths: avoids criticism, sparks public interest, treaties implemented, sanctions can compel compliance, UPR recommendations influential. Limitations: limited enforcement mechanisms, no legal obligation unless treaty ratified/member of organisation, can limit tailored laws, declarations/UPR recommendations not legally binding, conflicting international views.  
* **Lesson 7H: Parliament’s ability to make law – representative nature:** Parliament is representative, elected by people (s7/24 Const, *Constitution Act 1975 (Vic)*). Effectiveness relates to quality and addressing needs. Reflecting community: composition (diversity by gender, ancestry, age) impacts representation, MPs not required to reside in electorate. Regular elections (s28 Const for HoR duration) place pressure on MPs to represent constituents. Political parties offer choice. Independents can act in electorate's interest and influence major parties. Conscience votes allow disregard of party lines \[61 to freedom of political communication (*ACTV*, *Lange* cases), defining voting ability (*Roach* case). S28 Const requires regular elections (3-year term HoR). Strengths: HCA can declare invalid laws breaching principle, recognises implied rights, HCA is independent. Limitations: HCA only acts if case brought before it, standing requirement limits cases.  
* **Lesson 7J: The Constitution as a check on parliament – the separation of powers:** Principle ensures legislative, executive, judicial powers are separate (federal level) to prevent abuse. Legislative (s1) makes laws. Executive (s61) administers laws (Gov-Gen, in practice Cabinet). Judicial (s71) applies laws (courts/tribunals). Overlap exists between legislative/executive in practice (Cabinet members are MPs), but judiciary is separate. Reasons: prevent abuse, uphold rule of law, define body functions. HCA can declare laws ultra vires if outside powers. *Australian Communist Party v Commonwealth* case example. Strengths: constitutional check, minimises abuse, judiciary independence, legislature checks executive, HCA checks ultra vires laws, constitutionally guaranteed. Limitations: overlap limits internal check, challenging invalid laws requires standing/finance, Const only guarantees separation at fed level. \*690\] (limited application, *Brown v R* case); interstate trade/commerce (s92) (not absolute, not discriminatory *Cole v Whitfield* case); freedom of religion (s116 Cth) (limited to Cth, *Queensland Sikh knife* case); no discrimination state residence (s117) (*Goryl v Greyhound Australia* case). Strengths: enshrined/unchangeable by parl (via referendum), direct imposition on parl law-making. Limitations: 'just terms' undefined, s80 limited scope, s92 not absolute, s116 limited to Cth, requires HCA challenge/standing, limited number of rights.

**Chapter 8: The Victorian courts and the High Court in law-making** is outlined.

* **Lesson 8A: Statutory interpretation:** Courts give meaning to legislation when applying it to cases. Judges interpret to resolve disputes, clarifies/develops law, forms precedent. Reasons: clarify specific meaning (*Deing v Tarola*), reflect changing nature of words (*Attorney-General (Cth) v Kevin and Jennifer*), apply to unforeseen circumstances (AI/ChatGPT hypothetical excerpt, using the source notation `[i]` to indicate where the information originates within the text. I have bolded some key terms and used bullet points to present lists for clarity, as requested.

Please let me know how you would like to proceed with this source report.

Based on the sources provided and our conversation history, here is an analysis of how the sources collectively address a range of topics related to the VCE Legal Studies study design.

The sources collectively provide a comprehensive overview and detailed examination of key aspects of the Australian legal system, particularly focusing on law-making, constitutional principles, the court system, influences on law reform, and justice.

**1\. VCE Legal Studies Study Design Overview** The primary source details the VCE Legal Studies study design, outlining its structure, aims, rationale, and assessment requirements for Units 1 to 4\. It specifies the key knowledge and skills students are expected to develop. The study emphasizes understanding the rule of law, law-makers, legal institutions, constitutional principles, rights protection, and the Victorian justice system. It also introduces the principles of justice: fairness, equality, and access. The assessment structure, including School-assessed Coursework (SAC) and the end-of-year examination for Units 3 and 4, is clearly defined, with specific percentage contributions to the study score. Units are designed to involve at least 50 hours of instruction each.

**2\. Victorian and Commonwealth Parliaments** The sources describe the structure of both the Commonwealth and Victorian Parliaments as **bicameral**, consisting of two houses and the Crown.

* **Commonwealth Parliament:** Comprised of the King (represented by the Governor-General), the Senate (upper house), and the House of Representatives (lower house).  
* **Victorian Parliament:** Consists of the King (represented by the Governor), the Legislative Council (upper house), and the Legislative Assembly (lower house).

The roles of each component in law-making are detailed:

* **House of Representatives/Legislative Assembly:** Often referred to as the 'people's house' and where the government is formed. Their roles include initiating/making laws (especially money bills in the lower house), representing the people, scrutinizing government administration, controlling government expenditure, and acting as a house of review for bills initiated in the upper house.  
* **Senate/Legislative Council:** Often called the 'house of review' and the 'states' house' (Senate). Their primary role is scrutinizing bills from the lower house, but they can also initiate bills (except money bills), and represent the interests of states or regions. The composition of the upper house, particularly whether the government holds a majority, significantly impacts its ability to scrutinize legislation.  
* **The Crown (Governor-General/Governor):** Their main role in law-making is granting royal assent, the final approval for a bill to become law. They also have the rare power to withhold royal assent and are responsible for appointing the Executive Council.

The law-making process involves a bill passing through both houses (first, second, and third readings, with optional consideration in detail/committee of the whole) and receiving royal assent.

**3\. Division of Law-Making Powers** The Australian Constitution divides law-making powers between the Commonwealth and state parliaments.

* **Exclusive Powers:** Can only be exercised by the Commonwealth Parliament. Examples include defence, currency, and customs.  
* **Concurrent Powers:** Shared by both the Commonwealth and state parliaments. Examples include taxation, marriage, and trade.  
* **Residual Powers:** Powers that remained with the states and are not listed in the Constitution. Examples include criminal law, education, and public transport.

**4\. Section 109 of the Australian Constitution** Section 109 is designed to resolve conflicts between state and Commonwealth laws in areas of concurrent power. It states that if a state law is inconsistent with a Commonwealth law, the Commonwealth law prevails to the extent of the inconsistency, making the inconsistent part of the state law invalid. Its significance lies in restricting state parliaments' law-making powers in concurrent areas where Commonwealth law exists. However, it requires a court challenge for an inconsistent state law to be declared invalid, and if the Commonwealth law changes, the state law can come back into effect. The case of *McBain v Victoria* (2000) is provided as an example where section 109 was applied.

**5\. High Court of Australia** The High Court is established under section 71 of the Constitution and has the power to hear matters arising under or involving the interpretation of the Constitution. It acts as a guardian of the Constitution and can declare laws invalid if they go beyond parliament's law-making powers (*ultra vires*). Several significant High Court cases are discussed for their impact on the division of law-making powers and constitutional interpretation:

* *R v Brislan; Ex parte Williams* (1935): Interpreted section 51(v) ('postal, telegraphic, telephonic, and other like services') to include wireless broadcasting, shifting power regarding communication technology to the Commonwealth.  
* *Commonwealth v Tasmania* (Tasmanian Dams Case) (1983): Interpreted section 51(xxix) ('external affairs') to allow the Commonwealth to legislate on matters covered by international treaties, even if they fall under residual state powers, leading to a shift in the division of powers.  
* *Roach v Electoral Commissioner* (2007): Interpreted sections 7 and 24 (representative government) regarding the right to vote, finding a law banning all prisoners from voting invalid as it breached the principle of representative government. The High Court's independence from political influence is highlighted.

**6\. Constitutional Checks on Parliament** The Australian Constitution includes structures that check parliament's law-making power. These include:

* **Bicameral Structure:** The requirement for bills to pass both houses provides scrutiny, especially when the government lacks a majority in the upper house (hostile upper house). However, a government majority in both houses can lead to bills being 'rubber stamped' with less scrutiny.  
* **Separation of Powers:** Divides power between legislative (parliament), executive (administering laws), and judicial (courts) branches to prevent abuse. While the legislative and executive branches have overlap, the judiciary is independent, providing a check by interpreting laws and the Constitution.  
* **Express Protection of Rights:** Five specific rights are entrenched in the Constitution, limiting parliament's ability to make laws that infringe them. These are legally enforceable in the High Court. Limitations exist, such as some rights only applying to the Commonwealth or having narrow interpretations.  
* **Role of the High Court in Interpreting the Constitution:** By interpreting the Constitution, the High Court defines the scope of parliament's powers and can declare laws invalid if they are unconstitutional. It can also recognize implied rights, acting as a check.  
* **Requirement for a Double Majority in a Referendum:** Section 128 requires a national majority vote and a majority vote in a majority of states to change the Constitution. This makes constitutional change difficult, acting as a strong check on parliament by giving the final say to the people.

**7\. Doctrine of Precedent** The doctrine of precedent is a fundamental principle of common law where judges follow the legal reasoning (*ratio decidendi*) of superior courts in the same court hierarchy when deciding cases with similar material facts. It is based on the principle of *stare decisis* ('to stand by what has been decided'). Sources explain binding precedent (must be followed) and persuasive precedent (can guide, but not bound), including *obiter dicta* (comments made by the way). Courts can avoid applying precedent by distinguishing the facts. Superior courts can change precedent by overruling (previous case) or reversing (same case on appeal), while lower courts can disapprove. The doctrine ensures consistency and predictability in the legal system.

**8\. Statutory Interpretation** Statutory interpretation is the process by which courts give meaning to the words in legislation when applying them to a case. Reasons include clarifying ambiguous words, reflecting the changing nature of words, and applying legislation to unforeseen circumstances or technologies. Effects of statutory interpretation include the **creation of precedent** for future cases, broadening or narrowing the scope of the statute, and prompting legislative change if parliament disagrees with the court's interpretation or wishes to confirm it (codification). Cases like *Deing v Tarola* and the *Mabo Case* are prominent examples.

**9\. Relationship between Courts and Parliament** The sources highlight that courts and parliament have a **complementary role in law-making**. Features of their relationship include:

* **Supremacy of Parliament:** As the elected body, parliament is the supreme law-making body and can make or change laws (except High Court constitutional interpretations).  
* **Ability of Courts to Influence Parliament:** Courts can influence parliament through statutory interpretation, application of common law, and comments made in *obiter dicta*, prompting parliament to consider law reform.  
* **Codification of Common Law:** Parliament can enact legislation to confirm common law principles established by courts.  
* **Abrogation of Common Law:** Parliament can override common law decisions (except High Court constitutional decisions) by passing conflicting legislation.

**10\. Factors Affecting Parliament's Ability to Make Law** Sources discuss factors like the **bicameral structure** (already covered under constitutional checks), **international pressures**, and the **representative nature of parliament**.

* **International Pressures:** Influence from other countries and international organizations (like the UN, WTO, Amnesty International) through treaties, declarations, or scrutiny can pressure parliament to make or change laws to meet international standards. While international law isn't always directly enforceable domestically, failure to comply can lead to criticism and sanctions.  
* **Representative Nature:** Parliament is elected to represent the views and values of the people. Factors like compulsory voting, frequency of elections, and the diversity of elected members affect how well parliament represents the community and its ability to enact laws that reflect societal needs. Political parties and independents also influence law-making.

**11\. Factors Affecting Courts' Ability to Make Law** Sources discuss factors affecting the courts' ability to make law.

* **Doctrine of Precedent:** While enabling consistent law development, it can also limit courts, as they are bound by higher courts and must wait for relevant cases to be brought before them.  
* **Judicial Conservatism and Activism:** Judicial conservatism is a reluctance to make significant new law, deferring to parliament. Judicial activism is a willingness to develop common law, considering social/political factors, which can lead to significant legal changes.  
* **Costs and Time:** Legal proceedings can be expensive and time-consuming, particularly in higher courts, limiting people's ability to bring cases. This restricts the opportunities for courts to develop the law through case decisions.  
* **Requirement for Standing:** A party must have a 'special interest' and be directly affected by the issue to bring a case before the court. This filters frivolous cases but can prevent public interest cases from being heard, limiting law reform through the courts.

**12\. Influences on Law Reform** Ideas for new laws can originate from various sources. Sources detail ways individuals and groups can influence law reform:

* **Petitions:** Formal written requests to parliament, supported by signatures, to prompt action. They provide an objective measure of support but don't guarantee parliamentary action.  
* **Demonstrations:** Public gatherings to express views and raise awareness, aiming to attract the attention of law-makers. They can show public support but may be controversial.  
* **Using the Courts:** Individuals can influence law reform by challenging existing laws or bringing test cases, leading to common law development or statutory interpretation. This can be costly and requires standing.  
* **Media:** Traditional and social media can generate community interest and awareness, influencing public opinion and pressuring politicians. However, media can oversimplify complex issues or have political biases.  
* **Law Reform Bodies:** Independent bodies investigate the need for law reform and make recommendations. Examples include the **Victorian Law Reform Commission (VLRC)**, which reviews Victorian law upon reference from the Attorney-General or on minor matters, conducting research and consultation. **Royal Commissions** are high-level public inquiries into matters of public concern, established by the executive, with broad powers to gather evidence and report findings and recommendations. These bodies provide expert, independent advice, but their recommendations are not binding on parliament. Parliamentary Committees also play a role in scrutinizing bills and conducting inquiries.

**13\. Constitutional Reform** Constitutional reform involves changing the wording of the Australian Constitution. The process is outlined in section 128 and requires a **referendum**, which is a compulsory national vote. A proposed change must be approved by both Houses of Commonwealth Parliament and then receive a **double majority**: a majority of voters nationwide and a majority of voters in a majority of states (at least four). Factors affecting the success of a referendum are discussed. Significant referendums mentioned include the 1967 referendum regarding First Nations people (removing discriminatory clauses and allowing Commonwealth to legislate for them, resulting in a shift to concurrent power) and the 2023 referendum regarding an Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Voice.

**14\. Principles of Justice** The principles of justice \- **fairness, equality, and access** \- are fundamental to the VCE Legal Studies study. The study examines how the legal and justice systems (both criminal and civil) aim to achieve these principles and considers the difficulties faced by different groups (e.g., First Nations people, young people, people of low socioeconomic status) in achieving these principles when engaging with the justice system.

**15\. Civil and Criminal Justice Systems** Sources provide an overview of both the Victorian **Criminal Justice System** (Unit 1, Unit 3 AOS 1\) and the **Civil Justice System** (Unit 2, Unit 3 AOS 2). Topics covered include their aims (maintaining social order, protecting rights, resolving disputes), key concepts (e.g., presumption of innocence, burden/standard of proof, liability), institutions involved (courts, police, VLA, CLCs, VCAT, Consumer Affairs Victoria), roles of personnel (judge, jury, parties, legal practitioners), processes (trials, plea negotiations, alternative dispute resolution methods), outcomes (sanctions, remedies), and challenges in achieving the principles of justice (e.g., costs, time, cultural differences).

**16\. Human Rights** Unit 2 Area of Study 3 focuses on **Human Rights**. Sources discuss the meaning and development of human rights, including the Universal Declaration of Human Rights. They examine how rights are protected in Australia through various means, such as the Australian Constitution, the Victorian Charter of Human Rights and Responsibilities, statute law, and common law. Students are required to investigate a contemporary human rights issue in Australia, including relevant laws, conflicting attitudes, possible reforms, and a case study of an individual or group influencing the protection of the right.

In summary, the provided sources extensively cover the mandated curriculum for VCE Legal Studies, offering definitions, explanations, examples, case studies, and analyses of the institutions, principles, processes, and challenges within the Australian legal system. They collectively address topics ranging from the foundational aspects of law and the Constitution to specific areas like criminal and civil law, human rights, and mechanisms for law reform, enabling a deep understanding of the subject matter.

Drawing on the information in the sources, we can compare how different sources discuss key concepts related to law-making in Australia. Here is a comparison of the perspectives and details provided by the sources on the **Division of Law-making Powers**, the **Significance of Section 109**, the **Bicameral Structure**, and the **Separation of Powers**.

---

**1\. Division of Law-making Powers**

Multiple sources discuss how law-making powers are divided between the Commonwealth and state parliaments in Australia. This division is established by the Australian Constitution and is important to avoid abuses of power and share law-making responsibility. The powers are categorized into three main types: Exclusive, Concurrent, and Residual.

* **Exclusive Powers:** These powers can only be exercised by the Commonwealth Parliament. They are specifically mentioned in the Constitution, particularly in sections 51 and 52\. Some examples provided across the sources include currency, defence/armed forces, customs and border protection, overseas trade, foreign affairs, immigration and naturalisation, and Medicare.  
* **Concurrent Powers:** These are law-making powers that are shared by both the Commonwealth and the state parliaments. They are listed in section 51 of the Constitution. Examples include taxation, marriage and divorce, banking, trade, and postal, telegraphic, telephonic, and similar services.  
* **Residual Powers:** These are the powers that were left solely with the state parliaments at the time of Federation. They are not explicitly listed in the Australian Constitution. Examples include criminal law, education, public transport, medical procedures, road laws, police, and prisons. Source 401-729 notes that state parliaments require no 'head of power' to create legislation in residual areas, while the Commonwealth needs one for areas in s 51\.

While most sources agree on the definitions and common examples, Source 244-328 uses the term "Specific Powers" as an umbrella category for both exclusive and concurrent powers, primarily listed in s 51\. This specific terminology is not consistently used in the same way in the other sources when describing the primary categories of the division of powers.

**2\. Significance of Section 109**

The significance of Section 109 of the Australian Constitution is also discussed in several sources. This section is designed to resolve conflicts or inconsistencies that arise when both the Commonwealth and a state parliament make laws in an area of concurrent power.

The sources consistently state that Section 109 dictates that when a state law is inconsistent with a Commonwealth law, the Commonwealth law shall prevail, and the state law shall, to the extent of the inconsistency, be invalid. This means the Commonwealth law remains valid, and the conflicting part of the state law is no longer applicable.

Key aspects of its significance highlighted by the sources include:

* It acts as a **restriction on state parliaments**, as they must recognise that their powers are constrained in concurrent areas where the Commonwealth has already made law.  
* It provides a **common approach for resolving conflicts** between state and Commonwealth laws.  
* It does **not automatically operate**; a potentially inconsistent law needs to be challenged in court (specifically the High Court) for the state law to be declared invalid.  
* If the conflicting Commonwealth law is changed or removed, the state law provisions that were previously deemed invalid may come back into effect.  
* Source 134-153, 244-328, 382-388, and 401-729 mention the case of *McBain v Victoria* as a pertinent example illustrating the application and significance of Section 109\.

Source 244-328 uniquely presents the *McBain* case in the context of s 109 leading to a shift in the *division* of powers (from residual to concurrent). Other sources discussing *McBain* and s 109 primarily focus on the inconsistency resolution aspect or link Section 109 to the impact of High Court decisions on the division of powers more generally, as seen in cases like the Tasmanian Dams case.

**3\. Bicameral Structure**

The bicameral structure of parliament, having two houses or chambers that must approve bills, is discussed as a factor affecting parliament's ability to make law and as a means by which the Constitution acts as a check.

Sources describe both the Commonwealth Parliament (House of Representatives and Senate) and the Victorian Parliament (Legislative Assembly and Legislative Council) as bicameral, although Source 358-377 and 401-729 note there is no constitutional requirement for states to be bicameral. The requirement for bicameralism at the federal level is stated in section 1 of the Constitution. Most bills must pass both houses before becoming law.

The **composition of the upper house** significantly affects law-making ability and acts as a check:

* **Hostile Upper House:** This occurs when the government of the day does not hold a majority in the upper house. The balance of power is held by the opposition, minor parties, and independents. This composition is seen as increasing scrutiny and debate on bills, acting as a powerful check on government power. However, it can also slow down the law-making process and potentially skew outcomes if minor parties/independents need to be appeased. Sources 244-328, 358-377, and 401-729 discuss this scenario and its effects.  
* **Rubber Stamp Upper House:** This occurs when the government of the day holds a majority in both the lower and upper houses. Bills may not be scrutinised properly, potentially leading to a misuse of law-making power and limiting the upper house's role as a house of review. While less scrutiny is a limitation, some view this as efficient as law-making can occur faster. Sources 244-328, 358-377, and 401-729 also discuss this scenario and its effects.

Sources consistently evaluate the bicameral structure by weighing these dynamics. Strengths often include the second house acting as a review mechanism to prevent a government-dominated lower house from passing laws without adequate scrutiny, especially in a hostile scenario. Limitations arise when the upper house acts as a rubber stamp, reducing the effectiveness of the review, or when a hostile upper house excessively slows or hinders necessary law-making.

**4\. Separation of Powers**

The separation of powers is described as a principle established by the Australian Constitution that ensures the legislative, executive, and judicial powers remain separate. This principle is a key means by which the Constitution acts as a check on parliament in law-making. It aims to prevent abuses of power by ensuring no single body has the totalitarian power to make, interpret, and apply the law simultaneously.

The three powers are defined in the sources:

* **Legislative Power:** The power to make laws, vested in Parliament.  
* **Executive Power:** The power to maintain and administer the law and the business of government. This is technically vested in the Crown (exercised by the Governor-General) but in practice is exercised by the Cabinet (Prime Minister, senior ministers) and government departments.  
* **Judicial Power:** The power vested in courts and tribunals to enforce laws and resolve legal matters. This power is exercised by the High Court and other federal courts.

Sources highlight that, **in practice, the legislative and executive powers overlap** in the Australian system because Cabinet ministers are also members of parliament. This overlap can limit the ability of the legislature to effectively check the executive internally.

However, the **judiciary must remain completely independent** of the other two branches. This independence is a key strength of the separation of powers as a check on parliament. Sources explain that the independent judiciary, particularly the High Court, can check parliament's law-making power by declaring laws made outside their constitutional authority (*ultra vires*) invalid. Sources 244-328 and 401-729 specifically use the *Australian Communist Party v Commonwealth* case as an example of the judiciary checking parliament in this way.

Potential limitations discussed include the executive's role in appointing judges, which could potentially influence the judiciary's composition, and the fact that the constitutional separation primarily applies at the federal level, not necessarily binding at the state level. Sources like 244-328 and 401-729 also mention the requirement for standing, costs, and time as factors that limit the courts' ability to act as a check by challenging laws.

Drawing on the provided sources, we can build a comprehensive picture of how the **Australian Constitution acts as a check on parliament in law-making**, a key concept within VCE Legal Studies Unit 4, Area of Study 1\. The Constitution establishes Australia's parliamentary system and provides mechanisms to ensure that parliament does not make laws beyond its powers. These checks aim to prevent abuses of power and ensure responsible governance.

Here is a synthesis of information from the sources on the key constitutional checks on parliament:

**1\. The Bicameral Structure**

The Australian Constitution establishes a bicameral Parliament at the Commonwealth level, requiring two houses, the House of Representatives and the Senate. Similarly, the Victorian Parliament is bicameral, consisting of the Legislative Assembly and the Legislative Council. The requirement for a bicameral structure at the federal level is stated in section 1 of the Constitution. Most bills must pass both houses before becoming law.

The bicameral structure acts as a check through the role and composition of the upper house.

* **House of Review:** The Senate (and the Victorian Legislative Council) acts primarily as a house of review for bills initiated in the lower house. This review process involves scrutinising, debating, and potentially amending or rejecting proposed legislation. This increases scrutiny and ensures adequate debate and consideration.  
* **Composition of the Upper House:** The effectiveness of this check depends significantly on the composition of the upper house.  
  * **Hostile Upper House:** When the government of the day does not hold a majority in the upper house, the balance of power is held by the opposition, minor parties, and independents. This increases scrutiny and debate, acting as a powerful check on government power. However, it can also slow down the law-making process and may lead to bills being altered for specific interests that don't represent the broader population.  
  * **Rubber Stamp Upper House:** When the government holds a majority in both houses, bills may pass with ease and receive less scrutiny. This can limit the upper house's role as a house of review and potentially lead to inadequate consideration of bills. While efficient, it can mean conflicting community interests are missed.

The inclusion of a second house is a strength as it prevents the government from passing legislation that solely aligns with its political agenda.

**2\. The Separation of Powers**

The separation of powers is a principle established by the Australian Constitution that ensures the legislative, executive, and judicial powers remain separate. This doctrine aims to prevent abuses of power by ensuring no single body has the totalitarian power to make, interpret, and apply the law simultaneously. The Constitution explicitly lays out these powers in sections 1 (legislative), 61 (executive), and 71 (judicial).

* **Legislative Power:** The power to make laws, vested in Parliament.  
* **Executive Power:** The power to maintain and administer the law and the business of government. Technically vested in the King (exercised by the Governor-General) but in practice exercised by the Cabinet (Prime Minister, senior ministers) and government departments.  
* **Judicial Power:** The power vested in courts and tribunals to enforce laws and resolve legal matters. This power is exercised by the High Court and other federal courts.

While the principle mandates separation, the sources highlight an **overlap between the legislative and executive powers** in the Australian system because Cabinet ministers are also members of parliament. This overlap can limit the ability of the legislature to effectively check the executive internally.

However, the **judiciary must remain completely independent** of the other two branches. This independence is a key strength of the separation of powers as a check on parliament. The independent judiciary, particularly the High Court, can check parliament's law-making power by declaring laws made outside their constitutional authority (*ultra vires*) invalid. Sources specifically use the *Australian Communist Party v Commonwealth* case as an example of the judiciary checking parliament in this way. The High Court's ability to invalidate laws ensures that parliament does not legislate outside the scope of its exclusive and concurrent powers.

Limitations include the executive's role in appointing judges, which could potentially influence the judiciary's composition. The constitutional separation primarily applies at the federal level, not necessarily binding at the state level, though most states have embedded this principle. The costs and time involved in challenging laws in court also limit the courts' ability to act as a check.

**3\. Express Protection of Rights**

Express rights are specific human rights or legal entitlements that are explicitly stated and entrenched in the Australian Constitution. Being entrenched means they can only be amended or removed through a referendum process under section 128 of the Constitution, not by parliament alone. Express rights are legally enforceable, allowing individuals to challenge legislation that allegedly breaches these rights in the High Court, provided they have standing.

The five express rights mentioned in the sources are:

* Acquisition of property on ‘just terms’.  
* Trial by jury for Commonwealth indictable offences.  
* Interstate trade and commerce.  
* Freedom of religion.  
* No discrimination based on your state of residence.

These rights act as a check by imposing limits on parliament's law-making powers in certain areas. For instance, section 116 restricts the Commonwealth Parliament from creating laws regarding religion, such as establishing a state religion or imposing a religious test for office. The High Court's role in enforcing these rights allows for a judicial check on parliament.

Strengths of express rights include their entrenchment, which prevents parliament from legislating them away easily, and their enforceability in the High Court. However, sources note limitations such as the difficulty and cost of High Court action, the limited number and scope of the rights listed, and that some rights may not apply to state laws.

**4\. The High Court's Role in Protecting Representative Government**

The principle of representative government, where members of parliament are elected by the people to represent their views, is established and protected by sections 7 and 24 of the Australian Constitution. Section 7 requires senators to be "directly chosen by the people of the State," and section 24 requires members of the House of Representatives to be "directly chosen by the people of the Commonwealth". This principle enforces that parliament should establish laws that reflect the views and values of society.

The High Court acts as a guardian of the Constitution, interpreting its meaning and settling disputes, including those arising under sections 7 and 24\. In doing so, the High Court can declare laws invalid (*ultra vires*) if they go beyond parliament's powers or infringe upon constitutional principles like representative government.

The High Court has interpreted sections 7 and 24 in significant cases to protect the principle of representative government.

* **Implied Right to Freedom of Political Communication:** The High Court has recognised an implied right to freedom of political communication, interpreted from sections 7 and 24\. This allows Australians to access information about political candidates and parties, enabling informed voting and promoting a government that represents majority views. The *Australian Capital Television* case is cited as establishing this implied right.  
* **Right to Vote:** The High Court has also defined when the ability to vote in Commonwealth elections can and cannot be removed, ensuring a substantial majority of the population can select their representatives. The *Roach v Electoral Commissioner* case is a key example, where the High Court deemed a complete ban on prisoners voting unconstitutional as it was inconsistent with representative government under sections 7 and 24\. This decision restricted the Commonwealth's ability to pass laws regarding who may vote without good reason.

The High Court's independence is a strength, as decisions are made based on legal principles rather than political pressure. Its interpretations add flexibility to the Constitution. However, the High Court can only interpret the Constitution when a case is brought before it by a person with standing, and the costs and time involved can be significant limitations.

In summary, the Australian Constitution provides significant checks on the law-making power of parliament through its bicameral structure, the separation of powers (particularly the independent judiciary), the entrenchment and enforceability of express rights, and the High Court's role in protecting principles like representative government. These mechanisms, operating in conjunction, limit parliament's authority and promote responsible and constitutional law-making.
