Based on the provided sources, the **key knowledge** and **key skills** for **VCE Legal Studies Unit 4: The people, the law and reform, Area of Study 1: The people and the law-makers** are clearly outlined in the Victorian Curriculum and Assessment Authority (VCAA) Study Design and other study resources.

**Key Knowledge (Unit 4 AOS 1\)**

The key knowledge points focus on understanding how laws are made and the checks on parliamentary power at the Commonwealth and Victorian levels:

* **Parliament and the Australian Constitution:**  
  * The roles of the **Crown** and the **Houses of Parliament** (Victorian and Commonwealth) in law-making. This includes understanding the functions of the King's representatives (Governor-General and Governor), the House of Representatives, the Senate, the Legislative Assembly, and the Legislative Council.  
  * The **law-making powers** of the state and Commonwealth parliaments, including exclusive, concurrent and residual powers.  
  * The significance of **section 109 of the Australian Constitution**. This section deals with the resolution of inconsistencies between Commonwealth and state laws.  
  * One **High Court case** which has had an impact on state and Commonwealth law-making powers. Examples provided include *R v Brislan; Ex parte Williams* (1935) and *Commonwealth v Tasmania* (1983) (Tasmanian Dam case).  
  * Factors that affect the **ability of parliament to make law**, including:  
    * The **bicameral structure** of parliament.  
    * **International pressures**.  
    * The **representative nature** of parliament (representative government).  
  * The **means by which the Australian Constitution acts as a check on parliament** in law-making, including:  
    * The role of the **High Court in protecting the principle of representative government**.  
    * The **separation of the legislative, executive and judicial powers**.  
    * The **express protection of rights**.  
* **The Victorian courts and the High Court in law-making:**  
  * The reasons for, and effects of, **statutory interpretation**.  
  * Features of the **doctrine of precedent** including binding precedent, persuasive precedent, and the reversing, overruling, distinguishing, and disapproving of precedent.  
  * Factors that affect the **ability of courts to make law**, including:  
    * The doctrine of precedent.  
    * Judicial conservatism and judicial activism.  
    * Costs and time in bringing a case to court.  
    * The requirement for standing.  
  * Features of the **relationship between courts and parliament** in law-making, including:  
    * The supremacy of parliament.  
    * The ability of courts to influence parliament.  
    * The codification of common law.  
    * The abrogation of common law.

**Key Skills (Unit 4 AOS 1\)**

The key skills outline what students should be able to do with the knowledge they gain in this area of study:

* **Define and use legal terminology**.  
* **Discuss, interpret and analyse legal principles and information**.  
* **Explain the law-making powers** of the state and Commonwealth parliaments, using examples.  
* **Analyse the relationship between parliament and courts**.  
* **Explain the significance of section 109** of the Australian Constitution.  
* **Discuss the significance of one High Court case** which has had an impact on state and Commonwealth law-making powers.  
* **Discuss the ability of parliament and the courts to make law**.  
* **Evaluate the means by which the Australian Constitution acts as a check on parliament** in law-making.  
* **Synthesise and apply legal principles** to actual and/or hypothetical scenarios.

These key knowledge and skills form the basis for teaching, learning, and assessment in Unit 4 AOS 1\.

## **Part 5: Exam Preparation and Practice**

### **5.1 Guide to VCAA Task Words**

* **Deconstruct Question:** Identify Task Word, Key Concepts, Mark Allocation, Scope.  
* **Task Words Overview:**  
* *Identify/Outline/Describe:* Recall/state features concisely.  
* *Explain:* Detail how/why; link cause/effect.  
* *Distinguish:* Differences ONLY.  
* *Compare:* Similarities AND Differences.  
* *Analyse:* Break down; examine relationships/impacts (how/why).  
* *Discuss:* Explore multiple perspectives (pros/cons, strengths/weaknesses).  
* *Evaluate/Discuss Extent:* Make JUDGEMENT based on criteria (PoJ); weigh strengths/weaknesses; conclude.

### **5.2 Detailed Writing Frames & Outlines**

1. Discuss the extent to which / ability / appropriateness of...  
* **Meaning:** Examine and state arguments covering both sides of an issue or topic, focusing on ability/appropriateness/extent.

* **Structure:**

* **Opener (Optional, recommended for 10 marks):** State position clearly.

* *Examples:* "The \[legal concept\] achieves \[link to question\] to a limited/significant extent." OR "The \[legal concept\] is/is not appropriate to a limited/significant extent." OR (Specific VCAT Example) "The Victorian Civil and Administrative Tribunal (VCAT) is the most appropriate institution to resolve this dispute, to a significant extent."

* **Paragraph 1 (Supporting Arguments):**

* *Introduce Point:* "One way the \[legal concept\] would be appropriate is..." / "One way the \[legal concept\] achieves \[link to question\] is through..."

* *Explain:* "\[link to question\] is achieved because..." / "This leads to..."

* *Add Ideas:* "Similarly..." / "Additionally..." / "Another reason..."

* **Paragraph 2 (Conflicting/Limiting Arguments):**

* *Introduce Point:* "However, the \[legal concept\] is potentially limited..." / "However, the \[Legal concept\] may not be appropriate because..."

* *Explain:* "\[link to question\] is not achieved because..." / "This leads to..."

* *Add Ideas:* "Similarly..." / "Additionally..." / "Another reason..."

* **Conclusion:** Restate the extent/appropriateness.

* *Examples:* "Overall, the \[legal concept\] does/does not achieve \[link to question\] to a limited/significant extent because..." OR "Overall, the \[legal concept\] is/is not appropriate to a limited/significant extent because..."  
  ![][image1]

2. Evaluate

(Source: Skill & Drill PDF)

* **Meaning:** Make a judgment using information, considering supporting evidence for and against.

* **Structure:**

* **Opener (Optional, recommended for 10 marks):** State position clearly.

* *Examples:* "The \[legal concept\] achieves \[link to question\] to a limited/significant extent." OR "The \[legal concept\] does/does not... \[link to question\]." OR (Specific Example) "Judicial case management powers... do enhance the civil justice system to achieve the principles of justice to a significant extent."

* **Paragraph 1 (Benefits/Strengths/Positives):**

* *Introduce Point:* "One way the \[legal concept\] improves..." / "One way the \[legal concept\] achieves..."

* *Explain:* "\[link to question\] is achieved because..." / "This leads to..."

* *Add Ideas:* "Similarly..." / "Another reason..." / "Additionally..."

* **Paragraph 2 (Limitations/Weaknesses/Negatives):**

* *Introduce Point:* "However, the \[legal concept\] is potentially limited..." / "On the other hand, the \[legal concept\] may be constrained because..."

* *Explain:* "\[link to question\] is not achieved because..." / "This leads to..."

* *Add Ideas:* "Similarly..." / "Additionally..." / "Another reason why..."

* **Conclusion:** Provide overall conclusion/opinion.

* *Examples:* "Overall, the \[legal concept\] does/does not achieve \[link to question\] to a limited/significant extent because..." OR "Despite these limiting aspects, the \[legal concept\] does... \[link to question\] because..." OR "Although \[legal concept\] has some potential, ultimately it is limited because..."  
  ![][image2]

3. Analyse

(Source: Skill & Drill PDF / Question Outline PDF)

* **Meaning:** Study parts in detail to determine interrelations. Examine facts/issues, draw out implications/key elements. 'Unpack'. Focus on *how* and *why*.

* **Structure (Guided by Question):**

* Identify key components/factors related to the question's focus.

* Explain how components interrelate or connect.

* Draw out significance, consequences, impacts (positive/negative).

* Use analytical language (see Section 5.3 below).

* **Example Points (Analysis of High Court changing division of powers):**

* Comment on High Court's ability to interpret Constitution.

* Explain how interpretation impacts division of powers (use example).

* Provide possible limitations to this ability.

* Explain how limitation restricts the ability.

* **Example Points (Analysis of s7/s24 protecting people):**

* Explain how s7/s24 enshrine representative government.

* Use example of High Court interpretation providing further protection (e.g., implied freedom of political communication).

* Provide limitations (e.g., scope of freedom).

* Use example demonstrating limitations.![][image3]

4. Discuss

(Source: Skill & Drill PDF)

* **Meaning:** Present clear, balanced argument identifying issues, showing strengths/weaknesses or points for/against.  
* **Structure:**  
* **Paragraph 1 (Benefits/Strengths/Positives):**  
* *Introduce Point:* "One way the \[legal concept\] improves..." / "One way the \[legal concept\] achieves..."  
* *Explain:* "\[link to question\] is achieved because..." / "This leads to..."  
* *Add Ideas:* "Similarly..." / "Additionally..." / "Another reason..."  
* **Paragraph 2 (Limitations/Weaknesses/Negatives):**  
* *Introduce Point:* "However, the \[legal concept\] is potentially limited..." / "On the other hand..."  
* *Explain:* "\[link to question\] is not achieved because..." / "This leads to..."  
* *Add Ideas:* "Similarly..." / "Additionally..." / "Another reason why..."

![][image4]

5. Compare

(Source: Skill & Drill PDF)

* **Meaning:** Recognise similarities and differences between features/characteristics and their significance.

* **Structure:**

* **Paragraph 1 (Similarities):**

* *Introduce Similarity:* "The \[legal concept A\] involves... which is similar to \[legal concept B\] because..." / "\[Concept A\] and \[Concept B\] are similar in that..."

* *Use Comparative Language:* "Similarly", "Additionally", "Likewise", "Furthermore", "Equally".

* **Paragraph 2 (Differences):**

* *Introduce Difference:* "The \[legal concept A\] involves... which is different to \[legal concept B\] because..." / "While \[Concept A\] involves..., contrastingly, \[Concept B\] focuses on..." / "Additionally, unlike \[Concept A\], \[Concept B\] requires..."

* *Use Contrasting Language:* "In contrast", "However", "On the other hand", "Contrastingly", "Unlike", "Whereas".  
  ![][image5]

6. Distinguish

(Source: Skill & Drill PDF)

* **Meaning:** Explain points of *difference only*.

* **Structure:**

* Focus sentences on points of difference.

* *Examples:* "The \[legal concept A\] involves... which is different to \[legal concept B\] because..." / "While \[Concept A\] involves..., contrastingly, \[Concept B\] focuses on..." / "Additionally, unlike \[Concept A\], \[Concept B\] requires..."

* *Use Contrasting Language:* "In contrast", "However", "On the other hand", "Contrastingly", "Unlike", "Whereas".  
  ![][image6]

7. Explain (Detailed Outline)

(Source: Question Outline PDF)

* **Structure:**

* **SIGNPOST:** Write a sentence identifying the clear main point, using keywords from the question.

* **ELABORATE & EXAMPLE:** Give a specific, relevant example. Use legal terminology.

* **EXPLANATION:** Detail how the example supports the point. Consider effect/impact.  
  ![][image7]

8. Discuss / Evaluate (Detailed Outline)

(Source: Question Outline PDF)

* **Structure:**  
* **Deconstruct Question:** Identify ideas/concepts, keywords, command term, number of parts.  
* **Contention Statement:** State your position directly (especially for Evaluate).  
* **Signposts (MENU):** Plan the key points/arguments (e.g., 3-4 points for Discuss/Evaluate).  
* **Body Paragraph 1:** Signpost 1 \-\> Elaborate/Expand (relevant legal concepts/elements) \-\> Strengths/Positives \-\> Weaknesses/Negatives \-\> Limitations/Restrictions.  
* **Body Paragraph 2:** Repeat for Signpost 2\.  
* **Body Paragraph 3:** Repeat for Signpost 3\.  
* **Conclusion:** 1 sentence summary/judgement.  
* *Use Contrasting Vocab:* however, alternatively, on the other hand, etc.

![][image8]

9. Discuss to the Extent (Detailed VCAT Example Outline)

(Source: Question Outline PDF)

* **Question:** 'The Victorian civil and administrative tribunal (vcat) is always the most appropriate institution for resoloving civil disputes and achieving the principles of justice in Victoria'. Discuss the extent to which you agree with this statement (10 marks)  
* **Contention:** State extent of agreement (e.g., moderate extent), acknowledging other bodies (Courts, CAV) are also appropriate for certain disputes/principles.  
* **BP1: Jurisdiction and Appropriateness:** VCAT only appropriate within its jurisdiction; limited for large/complex disputes (e.g., class actions) where Courts are more suitable. Exclusive jurisdiction for some matters promotes access/expertise.  
* **BP2: Cost and Time \-\> Access, Fairness, Equality:** VCAT upholds access/equality via efficiency (shorter hearings, less formality) and cost (tiered fees, some free claims). Promotes equality by reducing impact of income disparity.  
* **BP3: Appeals \-\> Fairness:** Appeals from VCAT limited (question of law only), potentially affecting fairness if remedy/outcome unsatisfactory. Requires going to costly Courts.  
* **BP4: Binding Outcomes \-\> Fairness:** Final VCAT hearing orders are binding, upholding fairness. Mediation/Compulsory Conference outcomes only binding if Deed of Settlement signed.  
* **Concluding Sentence:** Summarise overall position on VCAT's appropriateness.

### **5.3 Analytical and Signposting Words**

Using varied language improves structure and analysis.

* **Analytical Words/Phrases (How/Why/Impact):**  
* *Cause/Effect:* Therefore, Consequently, As a result, This leads to, Thus, Hence, Because, The effect/impact is, This means that...  
* *Significance:* Significantly, Crucially, Importantly, Notably, The significance of this is, An implication is, This highlights/demonstrates...  
* *Relationship:* Relates to, Connects to, In relation to, With reference to...  
* *Elaborating:* Specifically, In particular, More particularly, To elaborate, This involves...  
* **Signposting Words/Phrases (Structure):**  
* *Introducing:* Firstly/Secondly, One way, Initially, To begin with, Regarding...  
* *Adding:* Additionally, Furthermore, Moreover, Similarly, Likewise, Also, In addition, Another reason...  
* *Contrasting:* However, On the other hand, In contrast, Alternatively, Unlike, Whereas, Conversely, Despite, Although, While, Nevertheless...  
* *Examples:* For example, For instance, This can be seen, As illustrated by...  
* *Concluding:* Overall, In conclusion, To summarise, Ultimately, Therefore, Thus, Hence...

### **5.4 Exam Technique Tips**

* **Time Management:** Allocate time per mark during revision and stick to it.  
* **Read Carefully:** Underline/highlight task words, key terms, scope. Address all parts.  
* **Use Legal Terminology:** Be precise and accurate.  
* **Apply to Scenarios:** Explicitly link legal concepts to facts provided. Use '1 mark \= 1 distinct point' as a rough guide.  
* **Structure Logically:** Use topic sentences, flow logically, use signposting. Conclude evaluations. Review VCAA examiner reports/examples.

Here is a detailed glossary for VCE Legal Studies Unit 4 Area of Study 1, "The people and the law-makers," drawing on the provided sources and our conversation history.

**Glossary for VCE Legal Studies Unit 4 Area of Study 1: The People and the Law-makers**

This glossary includes key legal terminology and concepts covered in Unit 4 Area of Study 1, which examines how Australia's laws are made and reformed by parliament and courts, the influence of the Australian Constitution on law-making, and factors affecting the ability of these institutions to make law.

**Key Terms and Concepts:**

* **Abrogation of Common Law:** The process of parliament overruling common law by creating a statute that is contrary to a decision of the courts. Parliament has the power to pass legislation that overrides most common law decisions, with the exception of High Court decisions on constitutional matters.  
* **Acquisition of Property on Just Terms:** An express right under **section 51(xxxi)** of the Australian Constitution. This section gives the Commonwealth Parliament the power to legislate about and acquire 'property on just terms from any State or person' for any purpose within its law-making powers. If this power is exercised, the Commonwealth must provide 'just terms', such as fair and reasonable compensation. Limitations exist, such as no specification on what constitutes 'just terms' and this section not applying to the states.  
* **Act of Parliament (Legislation/Statute Law):** A law made by parliament. A bill becomes an Act of Parliament after it has been agreed to by both houses of parliament and signed by the Governor-General (Commonwealth) or Governor (Victorian).  
* **Aims (of Study):** VCE Legal Studies aims include enabling students to understand and apply legal terminology and principles, apply legal principles to scenarios, analyse law-making institutions and influences on law reform, understand legal rights, analyse dispute resolution methods, and examine the justice system's ability to achieve the principles of justice.  
* **Australian Constitution:** A set of rules that establishes the nature, functions, powers, duties, and limits of government in Australia. It establishes the Commonwealth Parliament and High Court, sets out matters relating to the states, defines Commonwealth law-making powers, and provides a mechanism (referendum) to change it. It establishes law-making powers of state and Commonwealth parliaments and protects the Australian people through structures that check parliament in law-making.  
* **Balance of Power:** The support of minor parties or independents in a house of parliament that enables the government to form a majority and enact their law-making. This occurs when the government does not hold a majority in the upper house (hostile upper house).  
* **Bicameral Parliament/Structure:** A law-making body with two houses or chambers that must approve new bills or amendments to laws. The requirement for a bicameral structure is laid out in the respective constitutions of the Victorian and Commonwealth Parliaments. The inclusion of a second house can prevent the government from passing legislation that aligns solely with its political agenda.  
* **Bill:** A proposed law introduced in a parliament by a member of one of its houses. To become a law, a bill must be agreed to by both houses of parliament and signed by the Governor-General (Commonwealth) or Governor (Victorian).  
* **Binding Precedent:** Legal reasoning of a higher court that must be followed by all lower courts in the same court hierarchy where the material facts are similar. If a precedent is binding, a judge must follow it regardless of whether they agree with the legal reasoning.  
* **Broadening and Narrowing Statutes:** Effects of statutory interpretation. Courts can broaden the meaning of legislation, expanding its scope, or narrow the meaning, restricting the scope of the law.  
* **Cabinet:** A central decision-making body consisting of the prime minister (federal) and premier (state), alongside senior ministers, responsible for developing government policy and addressing issues of concern. Cabinet exercises executive powers in practice.  
* **Codification of Common Law:** When parliament makes laws to affirm previous decisions within common law. Parliament has the power to do this as it is the supreme law-maker.  
* **Commonwealth Parliament:** The federal bicameral parliament in Australia, consisting of the King (represented by the Governor-General), the Senate (upper house), and the House of Representatives (lower house).  
* **Concurrent Powers:** Law-making powers that are shared by both the Commonwealth and state parliaments. These are listed in **section 51** of the Australian Constitution. Examples include taxation, marriage, and divorce.  
* **Constitution:** A set of rules and principles that establishes the nature, functions, powers, duties, and limits of the government. Australia has a formal written constitution.  
* **Constitutional Monarchy:** A system of government in which the Crown is the Head of State, but elected representatives have the power to create laws.  
* **Constitutional Reform:** The process of changing the Australian Constitution. This requires a referendum.  
* **Constituent:** A person who votes for, and is then represented in government by, an elected official.  
* **Costs and Time (Courts):** Factors that affect the ability of courts to make law. Pursuing a claim in court can be very time-consuming due to case backlogs and preparation time. Costs can include legal representation fees, court fees, and jury fees. High costs can deter potential plaintiffs from initiating civil proceedings.  
* **Crown:** The British monarch, part of the Australian system of government through representatives (Governor-General at federal level, Governors at state level). The Crown's representatives grant royal assent and appoint the Executive Council.  
* **Crossbench:** The members of parliament who do not belong to the major political parties, who are either independents or members of minor political parties.  
* **Debate (Parliament):** Bills are debated in parliament during the second reading stage.  
* **Delegated Legislation (Secondary Legislation):** Rules and regulations made by secondary authorities (local councils, government departments, statutory authorities) that have the power to do so because of an enabling Act passed by parliament.  
* **Democratic Society:** An organised system of people living in a community, in which the laws and processes that govern people’s lives are created by elected representatives.  
* **Disapproving a Precedent:** An act whereby a lower court expresses its disapproval of a precedent established by a higher court through its written judgment, but is still bound to follow it. Disapproving a precedent does not change it.  
* **Distinguishing a Precedent:** A process by which a court can avoid following a binding precedent by identifying a sufficiently large difference between the material facts of the case at hand and the case where the precedent was set.  
* **Division of Powers:** The constitutional division of law-making powers between the Commonwealth and state parliaments. It divides powers into exclusive, concurrent, and residual. This is important to avoid abuses of power and share law-making responsibility.  
* **Doctrine of Precedent:** A rule in which judges must follow the reasons (ratio decidendi) for decisions given by superior courts in the same court hierarchy when deciding a case before them with similar material facts. It is based on the principle of 'stare decisis'.  
* **Double Majority:** A requirement for a referendum to be successful. It requires a majority of voters nationwide in favour, and also a majority of voters in a majority of states (at least four).  
* **Election:** A public voting process in which new representatives are chosen to be part of parliament, and from which a government is determined.  
* **Electorate:** A geographical area comprised of approximately 110,000 voters represented by a member of the Commonwealth Parliament. In Victoria, an electoral district has 46,000-56,000 eligible voters for the Legislative Assembly.  
* **Employability Skills:** The study offers opportunities for students to develop these, with specific examples provided in the Support materials.  
* **Executive Council:** A group of senior government ministers (including the prime minister/premier) with the role of advising the Crown’s representative on government matters, and approving secondary legislation.  
* **Executive Power:** The power vested in the King and exercised by the Governor-General (at the Commonwealth level), or Governor (at the state level), to maintain and administer the law and the business of government. In practice, this power rests with Cabinet and government departments.  
* **Exclusive Powers:** Law-making powers granted only to the Commonwealth Parliament by **section 51** and **section 52** of the Australian Constitution. State parliaments cannot make laws in these areas. Examples include currency, defence, and customs.  
* **Express Protection of Rights:** Rights that are explicitly stated (enumerated) and entrenched in the Australian Constitution. They can only be changed by a referendum. The five express rights are acquisition of property on just terms, trial by jury for Commonwealth indictable offences, interstate trade and commerce, freedom of religion, and no discrimination based on state residence.  
* **External Affairs Power:** Law-making power of the Commonwealth Parliament under **section 51(xxix)** of the Australian Constitution, allowing it to make laws with respect to external affairs. This power has been interpreted by the High Court to allow the Commonwealth to legislate in areas covered by international treaties.  
* **Factors Affecting Parliament's Ability to Make Law:** Include the bicameral structure of parliament, international pressures, and the representative nature of parliament.  
* **Factors Affecting Courts' Ability to Make Law:** Include the doctrine of precedent, judicial conservatism and activism, costs and time in bringing a case to court, and the requirement for standing.  
* **Fairness:** A principle of justice where all people can participate in the justice system, and its processes should be impartial and open.  
* **Freedom of Religion:** An express right under **section 116** of the Australian Constitution. The Commonwealth shall not make any law for establishing any religion, imposing religious observance, or prohibiting the free exercise of religion. Limitations exist, as it primarily limits the Commonwealth, not state parliaments.  
* **Governor (Victorian):** The Crown’s representative in the Victorian Parliament. The primary role is to grant royal assent.  
* **Governor-General (Commonwealth):** The Crown’s representative in the Commonwealth Parliament. The primary role is to grant royal assent.  
* **High Court of Australia (HCA):** Established by the Australian Constitution. It has jurisdiction under **section 75** and **section 76** to hear matters arising under the Constitution or involving its interpretation. The HCA acts as a guardian of the Constitution, interprets its meaning, settles disputes, and can declare laws invalid (ultra vires) if they go beyond parliament's law-making powers.  
* **Hostile Upper House:** Occurs when the government of the day does not hold a majority in the upper house, and the balance of power is held by the opposition, minor parties, and independents. This increases scrutiny on bills but can slow the law-making process.  
* **House of Representatives (Commonwealth Lower House):** The lower house of the Commonwealth Parliament, comprised of 151 members representing electorates across Australia. The political party or coalition with the majority of seats forms the government. Roles include initiating most new bills (especially money bills), determining the government, representing the people, and scrutinising government administration.  
* **Implied Right:** A right that is not explicitly outlined in the Australian Constitution but has been interpreted by the High Court to exist. An example is the implied right to freedom of political communication, interpreted from sections 7 and 24\.  
* **Initiating Legislation:** The process of introducing a proposed law (bill) into parliament. Most legislation is initiated in the lower house. Money bills can only be initiated in the lower house.  
* **International Declarations:** Non-binding agreements between two or more countries that establish aspirational rights and obligations. Unlike treaties, they are not subject to ratification. Despite not being legally bound, parliament may face international criticism for failing to uphold the principles set out in a declaration.  
* **International Pressures:** The influence that other countries and international organisations exert on parliaments to ensure the laws created comply with international standards. Pressures can come from other countries, intergovernmental organisations (like the UN), or non-governmental organisations.  
* **International Treaties:** Binding agreements between two or more countries or international organisations that create international rights and obligations. A country becomes bound after signing and ratifying the treaty. Parliament must pass legislation to give effect to treaty obligations within domestic law.  
* **Interstate Trade and Commerce:** An express right under **section 92** of the Australian Constitution. This section states that 'trade, commerce, and intercourse among the States... shall be absolutely free'. This is not an absolute right, and laws can restrict interstate movement if they are not discriminatory.  
* **Judicial Activism:** Refers to the willingness of judges to consider a range of social and political factors and their own opinions when interpreting the law and making decisions. Those who disapprove argue judges should not make substantial changes as they are not democratically elected.  
* **Judicial Conservatism:** An expression used when judges adopt a narrow interpretation of the law, avoiding major or controversial changes and not being influenced by political beliefs or community views. Conservative judges tend to interpret legislation narrowly.  
* **Judicial Power:** The power vested in courts and tribunals that enables them to enforce laws and resolve legal matters. At the Commonwealth level, this power is vested predominantly in the High Court. The judiciary must operate independently from the legislative and executive branches.  
* **Juries:** Have a role in criminal trials and civil trials. In a criminal trial, the jury determines the guilt of the accused.  
* **Just Terms:** Fair and reasonable compensation provided by the Commonwealth Parliament when acquiring property under **section 51(xxxi)**.  
* **Legislative Assembly (Victorian Lower House):** The lower house of the Victorian Parliament, comprised of 88 elected members representing electoral districts across Victoria. The political party with the majority of seats forms the government, led by the premier. Roles include initiating most new legislation (especially financial spending legislation), representing the people, reviewing legislation from the Legislative Council, and scrutinising government administration.  
* **Legislative Council (Victorian Upper House):** The upper house of the Victorian Parliament, comprised of 40 elected members representing eight regions across Victoria. It is often referred to as the 'house of review'. Roles include acting as a house of review, scrutinising government administration, and initiating bills (other than money bills).  
* **Legislative Power:** The power vested in parliament that enables it to make laws. At the Commonwealth level, this power is vested in the Commonwealth Parliament.  
* **Locus Standi:** See Standing.  
* **Material Facts:** The key facts or details in a legal case that were critical to the decision.  
* **Member of Parliament (MP):** An elected, political representative of people who live in a certain area, such as a district or state.  
* **Minor Parties:** Political parties that do not have elected representatives to win government but can place pressure on the government and target specific areas of law reform. They are small political parties.  
* **Money Bills:** Bills that relate to government expenditure or taxation. In the Commonwealth Parliament, all money bills must be initiated in the House of Representatives. In the Victorian Parliament, the Legislative Council is prevented from introducing legislation concerned with spending government funds.  
* **Obiter Dictum:** Latin term meaning 'said by the way'. These are comments made by the judge that do not necessarily contribute to the ratio decidendi but can be used as persuasive precedent. Disapproving a precedent is an obiter dictum.  
* **Overruling a Precedent:** Occurs when a different case is brought before a superior court at a later date, and they change the precedent set by a lower court or one of the same standing in an earlier case.  
* **Parliament:** A formal assembly of elected representatives that gathers to make laws for the people. Parliament is the supreme law-making body within its jurisdiction.  
* **Parliamentary Counsel:** Lawyers tasked with writing up legislation on the instructions and advice of members of parliament. They aim to draft legislation broadly to cover future circumstances but specifically enough to remove ambiguity.  
* **Persuasive Precedent:** Legal reasoning that can act as a guide for judges even though they are not bound to follow it. Examples include obiter dictum, decisions from lower courts or courts in different hierarchies.  
* **Petitions:** A formal written request to the government to take some course of action or implement specific law reform, usually with collected signatures. They are the only way individuals can present concerns 'directly' before parliament.  
* **Political Party:** An organisation comprised of members of similar ethos and worldviews with the primary aim to compete in elections to achieve societal and political goals.  
* **Premier (Victorian):** The leader of the Victorian state government.  
* **Presumption of Innocence:** A fundamental principle of criminal law that an accused is presumed innocent until proven guilty beyond reasonable doubt.  
* **Principles of Justice:** For VCE Legal Studies, these are fairness, equality, and access.  
  * **Fairness:** All people can participate in the justice system, and its processes should be impartial and open.  
  * **Equality:** All people engaging with the justice system and its processes should be treated the same way; if the same treatment creates disparity or disadvantage, adequate measures should be implemented.  
  * **Access:** All people should be able to engage with the justice system and its processes on an informed basis.  
* **Promoting Legislative Change:** An effect of statutory interpretation. Parliament may amend legislation in response to statutory interpretation to provide clarity or confirm a court decision.  
* **Proportional Representation:** The Senate is elected by proportional representation. The proportional voting system allows a number of people to be elected from the same region or state, meaning each candidate needs relatively fewer votes to win a seat.  
* **Quantum Translocator:** A hypothetical teleportation device used in an example to illustrate statutory interpretation.  
* **Ratio Decidendi:** Latin phrase meaning 'the reason for the decision'. This is the legal reason for the decision and is the binding part of a judgment that must be followed by lower courts.  
* **Ratification:** The formal agreement or undertaking by a country to be bound by the terms of an international treaty.  
* **Reasons for Statutory Interpretation:** Include clarifying the specific meaning of words, the changing nature of words, and applying legislation to unforeseen circumstances.  
* **Referendum:** A compulsory national vote on a proposed change to the wording of the Australian Constitution, as outlined in **section 128**. A double majority is required for success.  
* **Region (Victorian Legislative Council):** One of the eight, larger divisions of Victoria that Legislative Council members are elected to represent.  
* **Remand:** The legal status of an accused when they are held in custody awaiting trial.  
* **Representative Government:** A system of government wherein the people choose elected representatives to parliament to make laws on their behalf. The principle is established and protected by **sections 7** and **24** of the Australian Constitution.  
* **Representative Nature of Parliament:** Parliament's duty to voters and the community to represent their interests through law-making, underpinned by the election system. It is affected by who is nominated for election and the diversity of parliament.  
* **Residual Powers:** Law-making powers that were left solely with the state parliaments at federation. They are not explicitly listed in the Australian Constitution. Examples include criminal law, medical procedures, road laws, education, and public transport.  
* **Responsible Government:** The principle that the party or coalition with a majority in the lower house forms the government, and the executive council is responsible to parliament.  
* **Reversing a Precedent:** Occurs when a case is appealed and the superior court changes the precedent set by a lower court. A new precedent is established by the superior court. This differs from overruling, which happens in a different case.  
* **Royal Assent:** The formal and final approval granted by the monarchy, or its representative (Governor-General or Governor), for a proposed piece of legislation. It is a necessary final step for a bill to become law.  
* **Rubber Stamp (Upper House):** A situation where the government holds a majority of seats in both the upper and lower houses, meaning bills may not be scrutinised properly. Bills introduced in the lower house are unlikely to be contested by the upper house.  
* **Scope of Study:** VCE Legal Studies examines the institutions and principles essential to the Australian legal system, including the rule of law, law-makers, legal institutions, the relationship between people and the Constitution, the protection of rights, and the Victorian justice system.  
* **Scrutinising Government/Legislation:** The process of closely examining bills or government administration. In the Commonwealth Parliament, the Senate's primary role is to scrutinise bills from the House of Representatives. In the Victorian Parliament, the Legislative Council's primary role is also to act as a house of review.  
* **Secondary Legislation:** See Delegated Legislation.  
* **Section 109:** A section of the Australian Constitution designed to resolve inconsistencies between state and Commonwealth laws in areas of concurrent law-making powers. If a state law is inconsistent with a Commonwealth law, the latter prevails to the extent of the inconsistency, and the state law is invalid to that extent. It can act as a restriction on state parliaments.  
* **Sections 7 and 24:** Sections of the Australian Constitution that establish the composition of the Senate and the House of Representatives, stipulating that members are to be 'directly chosen by the people' through regular elections. They enforce the principle of representative government.  
* **Senate (Commonwealth Upper House):** The upper house of the Commonwealth Parliament, comprised of 76 elected members (12 from each state, 2 from each territory). It is often referred to as the 'states' house' or the house of review. Roles include acting as a house of review, allowing for equal representation of states, scrutinising bills, and initiating bills (other than money bills).  
* **Separation of Powers:** A principle established by the Australian Constitution that ensures the legislative, executive, and judicial powers remain separate. It aims to prevent abuses of power. In Australia, the legislative and executive powers overlap in practice, but the judiciary is independent.  
* **Significance of High Court Cases:** Refers to the impact a High Court case interpreting the Australian Constitution has on state and Commonwealth law-making powers. Examples include the Brislan case (wireless sets/telecommunications) and the Tasmanian Dams case (external affairs/international treaties).  
* **Significance of Referendums:** Refers to the impact a referendum has on changing the Australian Constitution. An example is the 1967 referendum which changed sections 51(xxvi) and removed 127, altering law-making powers regarding Indigenous Australians.  
* **Special Interest:** A requirement for a party to have standing in court. The party must be affected by the issue more than any member of the general public.  
* **Standing:** The requirement that, in order to bring a case to court, an individual or group must be affected by, or have a special interest in, the issues involved in the case.  
* **Stare Decisis:** Latin term meaning 'to stand by what has been decided'. It is a basic principle underlying the doctrine of precedent.  
* **Statute Law:** See Act of Parliament.  
* **Statutory Interpretation:** A process whereby the courts give meaning to the words in legislation when applying the legislation to a case. Judges interpret statutes to resolve cases, clarify ambiguous words, reflect changing meanings, and apply laws to unforeseen circumstances. It establishes a precedent for future cases.  
* **Supremacy of Parliament:** The legal concept that parliament has the freedom to make, amend, or abolish laws (subject to constitutional limitations) and is supreme over other arms of government. Parliament can confirm or cancel common law decisions (except High Court constitutional decisions).  
* **Trial by Jury (Commonwealth Indictable Offences):** An express right under **section 80** of the Australian Constitution. It requires that the trial on indictment for any offence against a Commonwealth law shall be by jury. This right is limited, as it only applies to Commonwealth indictable offences, not state ones or summary offences.  
* **Ultra Vires:** Latin term meaning 'beyond the powers'. Used to describe an act by a government body or corporation that requires legal authority but is done without it. Courts may declare laws ultra vires if they go beyond parliament's law-making powers.  
* **United Nations (UN):** An intergovernmental organisation aimed at promoting peace, human rights, and international cooperation. Australia is a founding Member State. The UN Charter is an international treaty binding on Australia. The UN's Universal Periodic Review process can recommend law reform to Member States.  
* **Victorian Law Reform Commission (VLRC):** Victoria's leading independent law reform organisation that reviews, researches, and makes recommendations to Victorian Parliament about possible changes to laws. Roles include inquiry (examining Attorney-General references), investigation (minor issues), monitoring law reform activity, and education.  
* **Victorian Parliament:** Consists of the King (represented by the Governor), the Legislative Council (upper house), and the Legislative Assembly (lower house).  
* **Victorian Study Design:** The VCE Legal Studies Study Design, updated periodically, which outlines the curriculum for the study.  
* **Voting 'Along Party Lines':** When members of a political party vote in the same way to pass bills, especially when their party holds a majority. This can occur even if the vote does not represent constituents' views.  
* **World Trade Organisation (WTO):** An international body comprising 164 nations that determines international trade rules. It can authorise sanctions against member countries breaching trade agreements.

Based on the information in the sources, here are some ideas for interactive charts or study materials that could enhance understanding of VCE Legal Studies Unit 4, Area of Study 1: "The people and the law-makers":

1. **Interactive Division of Powers and Section 109 Visualizer**

   * **Concept:** This visual tool would illustrate the allocation of law-making powers in Australia and how inconsistencies between state and Commonwealth laws are resolved.  
   * **Content Visualization:** Start with a main diagram showing the **Commonwealth Parliament** and **State Parliaments** side-by-side. Sections could expand or contract to display:  
     * **Exclusive Powers:** Powers held only by the Commonwealth Parliament, with examples like defence, currency, and customs.  
     * **Concurrent Powers:** Powers shared by both Commonwealth and state parliaments, with examples such as taxation, marriage, and trade.  
     * **Residual Powers:** Powers left solely with the state parliaments, with examples like education, criminal law, and public transport.  
   * **Interactive Element:** Clicking on the 'Concurrent Powers' section could trigger a pop-up or animation explaining the significance and operation of **Section 109 of the Australian Constitution**. It could explain that when a state law is inconsistent with a Commonwealth law on the same topic, the Commonwealth law prevails to the extent of the inconsistency, and the state law is invalid. It should also highlight that this requires a case to be brought before the courts, usually the High Court, to declare the state law invalid.  
   * **Case Integration:** Incorporate relevant High Court cases like **R v Brislan** or **Commonwealth v Tasmania (Tasmanian Dams)** as examples of how the High Court's interpretation of the Constitution (specifically the 'other like services' power under s 51(v) and the 'external affairs' power under s 51(xxix), respectively) has impacted the division of powers by broadening Commonwealth law-making ability in areas previously considered residual or narrower concurrent powers. Clicking on the case name could show a summary of the facts, legal issue, decision, and significance.  
2. **Comparative Parliament Structure and Roles Chart**

   * **Concept:** This interactive chart would compare the structure, composition, and law-making roles of the Commonwealth and Victorian Parliaments.  
   * **Content Visualization:** A table could be used with columns for **Commonwealth Parliament** and **Victorian Parliament**, and rows for key components:  
     * **Crown Representative:** Governor-General (Commonwealth) vs. Governor (Victoria). Include their role in granting Royal Assent.  
     * **Upper House:** Senate (Commonwealth) vs. Legislative Council (Victoria). Detail composition (members, states/regions represented, term length). Highlight key roles like acting as a house of review and scrutinizing bills. Mention their ability to initiate bills (excluding money bills).  
     * **Lower House:** House of Representatives (Commonwealth) vs. Legislative Assembly (Victoria). Detail composition (members, electorates/districts, term length). Highlight key roles like initiating and making laws, determining government, and representing the people. Mention the initiation of money bills in the lower house.  
   * **Interactive Element:** Users could click on each house to see detailed descriptions of its specific roles and functions in the law-making process, drawing information from the sources. An interactive overlay could explain the **bicameral structure** and how the composition of the upper house (hostile vs. rubber stamp) can affect law-making speed and scrutiny. Include examples of composition like the current Senate or Victorian Legislative Council.  
3. **Doctrine of Precedent and Statutory Interpretation Case Explorer**

   * **Concept:** This material would explore how courts make law through precedent and interpreting statutes, using specific case examples.  
   * **Content Visualization:** One section could focus on the **Doctrine of Precedent**. Define it and explain key principles like *stare decisis* ('to stand by what has been decided'), *ratio decidendi* (the binding reason for the decision), and *obiter dictum* (comments made 'by the way'). Visualize the court hierarchy showing how precedent flows (binding vs. persuasive). Detail ways judges can avoid following precedent, such as distinguishing, reversing, overruling, and disapproving, providing brief definitions for each. Include relevant cases demonstrating these concepts (e.g., **Donoghue v Stevenson** and **Grant v Australian Knitting Mills** for persuasive/binding precedent, **Trigwell** for disapproving, **Imbree v McNeilly** for overruling).  
   * **Interactive Element:** Users could click on terms like *ratio decidendi* or 'distinguishing' to see definitions and examples. An interactive element could ask users to determine if a precedent is binding or persuasive based on hypothetical facts and the court hierarchy.  
   * **Content Visualization:** A separate section would cover **Statutory Interpretation**. Explain the **reasons** judges interpret statutes (e.g., clarifying ambiguous words, accounting for changing nature of words, applying laws to unforeseen circumstances). Explain the **effects** of interpretation (e.g., creation of precedent, broadening or narrowing the statute's application, prompting legislative change).  
   * **Case Integration:** Include case studies that demonstrate statutory interpretation and its effects, such as **Deing v Tarola** (interpreting 'regulated weapon' and narrowing the statute), **Attorney-General (Cth) v Kevin and Jennifer** (interpreting 'man' and reflecting changing societal values), **Carr v Western Australia** (interpreting 'interview' and broadening the statute), **Mabo v Queensland (No. 2\)** (leading to the **Native Title Act 1993 (Cth)**). Clicking on a case could reveal the statute, the interpreted word/phrase, the context, and the outcome/effect.  
4. **Constitution as a Check Interactive Diagram**

   * **Concept:** This diagram would visually represent how the Australian Constitution limits Parliament's law-making power.  
   * **Content Visualization:** A central node could represent the **Parliament's Law-making Power**. Arrows or connecting lines could extend outwards to different 'check' mechanisms established by the Constitution:  
     * **Bicameral Structure:** As discussed in idea 2, the requirement for two houses acts as a check, especially when the upper house is hostile.  
     * **Separation of Powers:** Show the three branches (Legislative, Executive, Judicial) and explain how the independence of the judiciary, particularly the High Court, acts as a check by being able to declare laws *ultra vires* (beyond power). Highlight the overlap between Legislative and Executive in practice as a limitation on this check.  
     * **Express Protection of Rights:** List the five constitutionally entrenched rights (acquisition on just terms, trial by jury for Commonwealth indictable offences, interstate trade and commerce, freedom of religion, no discrimination based on state residence). Explain that laws infringing these rights can be challenged in the High Court. Note the limitation that most apply only to the Commonwealth or have limited scope.  
     * **Role of the High Court in Protecting Representative Government:** Explain how the High Court interprets sections 7 and 24 of the Constitution, which require MPs to be 'directly chosen by the people'. Use the **Roach v Electoral Commissioner** case as an example of the High Court striking down a law that was inconsistent with the principle of representative government by restricting voting rights.  
     * **Requirement for a Double Majority in a Referendum (s 128):** Explain that changing the Constitution's wording requires approval from both Houses of Parliament *and* a **double majority** (majority of voters nationwide and a majority of voters in a majority of states). This acts as a significant check by making constitutional change difficult and requiring public approval.  
   * **Interactive Element:** Clicking on each check mechanism could expand to provide detailed explanations, relevant constitutional sections, limitations, and case examples, drawing directly from the source material.

These interactive materials could serve as dynamic study tools, allowing students to visually explore complex legal concepts, understand relationships between different parts of the legal system, and delve into specific examples and case studies provided in the sources.

Okay, here are some practice questions for VCE Legal Studies Unit 4, Area of Study 1: "The people and the law-makers", drawing on the information provided in the sources. These questions cover the key knowledge and skills outlined for this area of study.

**Practice Questions for Unit 4, Area of Study 1**

1. **Explain the role of the Governor-General in the Commonwealth law-making process.** (3 marks)  
2. **Describe the primary role of the Senate in the Commonwealth Parliament.** (3 marks)  
3. **Explain one role of the Legislative Assembly in the Victorian Parliament.** (3 marks)  
4. **Distinguish between exclusive and concurrent law-making powers as outlined in the Australian Constitution, using an example for each.** (4 marks)  
5. **Describe residual law-making powers and provide one example.** (3 marks)  
6. **Explain the significance of Section 109 of the Australian Constitution.** (3 marks)  
7. **'Section 109 of the Australian Constitution restricts the law-making abilities of the state parliaments.' Discuss the extent to which you agree with this statement.** (6 marks)  
8. **Describe how Sections 7 and 24 of the Australian Constitution protect the principle of representative government.** (3 marks)  
9. **Explain how the separation of powers acts as a check on the law-making of parliament.** (3 marks)  
10. **Outline one express right entrenched in the Australian Constitution.** (2 marks)  
11. **Explain how the express protection of rights acts as a check on parliament in law-making.** (3 marks)  
12. **Evaluate the express protection of rights as one way in which the Australian Constitution acts as a check on parliament in law-making.** (6 marks)  
13. **Analyse how the High Court, in protecting the principle of representative government, acts as a check on parliament in law-making.** (4 marks)  
14. **Explain the significance of one High Court case that has impacted the division of law-making powers.** (4 marks)  
15. **Using one High Court case, analyse the impact of the High Court's interpretation of the Australian Constitution on state and Commonwealth law-making powers.** (6 marks)  
16. **Explain one reason why a court may need to interpret a statute.** (3 marks)  
17. **Outline two effects of interpretation of statute by judges.** (4 marks)  
18. **Explain the significance of the doctrine of precedent in the Australian legal system.** (3 marks)  
19. **Distinguish between binding precedent and persuasive precedent.** (3 marks)  
20. **Describe one way the Magistrates' Court of Victoria may not be bound by a decision of the County Court of Victoria.** (2 marks)  
21. **Outline two factors that affect the ability of courts to make law.** (4 marks)  
22. **Explain how the principle of parliamentary supremacy affects the relationship between courts and parliament in law-making.** (3 marks)  
23. **Describe one way courts can influence parliament in law-making.** (3 marks)  
24. **Distinguish between the codification and abrogation of common law, using examples.** (4 marks)  
25. **Analyse the relationship between parliament and the courts in law-making.** (6 marks)

**Scenario-Based Questions:**

26. **The Victorian Parliament passes a law regulating certain online gambling activities, which have recently become prevalent through new technologies. The Commonwealth Parliament had previously passed a law addressing the broader issue of online financial transactions. A challenge is brought to the High Court regarding the validity of the Victorian law. With reference to relevant law-making powers and the Australian Constitution, analyse how the High Court might resolve this inconsistency.** (6 marks)  
27. **A state passes a law regarding environmental protection in an area also covered by an international treaty Australia has ratified. Explain how a challenge to this state law in the High Court, citing the Commonwealth's external affairs power (s 51(xxix)), might impact the division of law-making powers, referring to *Commonwealth v Tasmania*.** (6 marks)  
28. **A recent Commonwealth law imposes a significant tax on certain types of religious organisations, effectively making their operation extremely difficult. Discuss the extent to which this law might be challenged under the Australian Constitution, referring to relevant constitutional checks.** (8 marks)

These questions should provide a good starting point for practicing the concepts in Unit 4 AOS 1\. Good luck with your studies\!

Based on the information provided in the sources, here is a list of important case studies relevant to VCE Legal Studies, particularly focusing on Unit 4, Area of Study 1, "The people and the law-makers". The sources also mention relevance to Unit 2, Area of Study 3 and Unit 3 & 4 more broadly.

Here are some important case studies:

* ***Commonwealth v Tasmania*** **(1983) (The Tasmanian Dams Case)**:

  * **Facts:** The Tasmanian Parliament passed a law to build a dam on the Gordon River. The area was listed on the UNESCO World Heritage List, a treaty Australia had ratified. The Commonwealth Parliament then passed a law prohibiting the dam's construction, relying on its 'external affairs' power.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The High Court had to interpret the words 'external affairs' in Section 51(xxix) of the Constitution and determine if the Commonwealth could legislate on an area (dam construction) that was previously considered a state residual power, to fulfill international treaty obligations.  
  * **Significance:** The High Court held that the external affairs power allowed the Commonwealth to legislate to fulfill its obligations under an international treaty. This meant the Commonwealth law prevailed over the inconsistent state law under **Section 109**, resulting in a **shift in the division of law-making powers from the states to the Commonwealth**. This case interpreted Section 51(xxix) to include giving effect to international treaties, potentially allowing the Commonwealth to legislate in areas previously considered state residual powers.  
* ***R v Brislan; Ex parte Williams*** **(1935) (The Brislan Case)**:

  * **Facts:** The Commonwealth passed a law requiring owners of wireless sets to hold a licence. The defendant was charged for not having a licence and challenged the law's validity, arguing the Constitution didn't give the Commonwealth power over radio broadcasting.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The High Court had to interpret the meaning of 'postal, telegraphic, telephonic, and other like services' in Section 51(v) of the Constitution to determine if it included radio broadcasting.  
  * **Significance:** The High Court decided that broadcasting to a wireless set was a form of telephonic service or a 'like service' within Section 51(v), confirming the Commonwealth Parliament had power to legislate in this area. This case **changed the division of law-making powers by extending the Commonwealth's power**, resulting in a **shift from the states to the Commonwealth**. It made wireless sets an area of concurrent power and suggested that the phrase 'other like services' was designed to cover new developments. This has influenced subsequent interpretations regarding television and potentially the internet.  
* ***McBain v State of Victoria*** **(2000)**:

  * **Facts:** Victorian law restricted IVF treatment to married or de facto couples. Commonwealth law made it unlawful to discriminate based on marital status when providing services. A doctor challenged the Victorian law's validity on behalf of a single woman.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The court needed to decide if the Victorian law was inconsistent with the Commonwealth law.  
  * **Significance:** The court found the Victorian legislation violated the Commonwealth Sex Discrimination Act 1984 (Cth) and was therefore **invalid under Section 109 of the Constitution**. This demonstrated that state and Commonwealth laws must remain consistent.  
* ***Croome v. Tasmania*** **(1997)**:

  * **Facts:** Parts of the Tasmanian Criminal Code criminalised certain sexual conduct. This was challenged as being inconsistent with a Commonwealth law based on the external affairs power relating to an international human rights covenant.  
  * **Significance:** The High Court agreed there was an inconsistency and **applied Section 109 to invalidate the relevant parts of the Tasmanian law**. This case highlighted how a residual power (discrimination regarding sexual activities) could become concurrent and showed an increase in Commonwealth power at the expense of the states.  
* ***Roach v. Electoral Commissioner*** **(2007)**:

  * **Facts:** A Commonwealth law banned all prisoners from voting. A prisoner serving a sentence challenged this, arguing it was inconsistent with the principle of representative government under Sections 7 and 24 of the Constitution.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The High Court interpreted Sections 7 and 24 to clarify the constitutional protection of the right to vote.  
  * **Significance:** The High Court held the complete ban on prisoners voting was unconstitutional as it was inconsistent with the principle of representative government. The decision affirmed a **structural right of Australian adults to vote** and demonstrated how the High Court, in protecting representative government, acts as a check on parliament in law-making.  
* ***Mabo v Queensland (No. 2\)*** **(1992) (The Mabo Case)**:

  * **Facts:** Indigenous activists challenged the principle of 'terra nullius' (land of no-one) which had been applied to Australia since British colonisation, claiming ownership rights over their traditional land.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The High Court had to determine whether First Nations Australians had 'native title' to the land.  
  * **Significance:** The High Court held that 'terra nullius' was a legal fiction and that native title could exist. This decision **overturned a long-standing common law principle** and was an example of judicial activism. The Commonwealth Parliament **codified the principle of native title** through the *Native Title Act 1993 (Cth)*, showing the relationship between courts and parliament in law-making.  
* ***Deing v Tarola*** **(1993)**:

  * **Facts:** A person was charged for possessing a studded belt, which the police considered a 'regulated weapon' under Victorian law.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The court had to interpret the meaning of 'regulated weapon' in the *Control of Weapons Act 1990 (Vic)* to determine if studded belts were included.  
  * **Significance:** The court **interpreted and narrowed the definition of 'regulated weapon'**, finding the belt did not fit. This case demonstrates the **reason for and effect of statutory interpretation**.  
* ***Attorney-General (Cth) v Kevin and Jennifer*** **(2003)**:

  * **Facts:** The validity of a marriage between a transgender man (Kevin) and a woman (Jennifer) was challenged, arguing Kevin was not a 'man' for the purposes of the *Marriage Act 1961 (Cth)*.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The Family Court had to interpret the meaning of the word 'man' in the *Marriage Act*.  
  * **Significance:** The court held that 'man' should be given a contemporary, everyday meaning, including transgender men, and declared the marriage valid. This shows how **statutory interpretation can reflect the changing nature of words**.  
* ***Donoghue v Stevenson*** **(1932)**:

  * **Facts:** A woman found a decomposed snail in a bottle of ginger beer and became ill. She sued the manufacturer for negligence.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The court had to determine if the manufacturer owed a duty of care to the final consumer.  
  * **Significance:** The British House of Lords found in favour of the plaintiff, **establishing the law of negligence through its ratio decidendi**. This case is often used as an example of **ratio decidendi and binding/persuasive precedent**, influencing the development of negligence law in Australia.  
* ***State Insurance Commission v Trigwell*** **(1979)**:

  * **Facts:** A car accident involving straying sheep led to a case questioning whether a landowner owed a duty of care for their stock on the highway. The High Court followed an old English common law precedent.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The case involved applying and considering the relevance of an existing common law precedent.  
  * **Significance:** The High Court followed the outdated precedent, demonstrating **judicial conservatism**, and suggested law-making in this area should be left to parliament. This case is often cited as an example of **disapproving a precedent** and **parliament subsequently abrogating common law** by passing the *Wrongs (Animals Straying on Highways) Act 1984* (Vic).  
* ***Australian Communist Party v Commonwealth*** **(1951)**:

  * **Facts:** The Commonwealth Parliament passed a law banning the Communist Party of Australia and seizing its property.  
  * **Legal Issue:** The Communist Party challenged the law's validity, arguing the Commonwealth did not have the power to pass it.  
  * **Significance:** The High Court ruled the legislation was invalid. This case exemplifies the importance of the **independence of the judiciary** under the separation of powers principle, showing the court acting as a check on parliament's law-making power.

These cases illustrate various key concepts within VCE Legal Studies Unit 4 Area of Study 1, including the division of powers, constitutional checks on parliament, the roles of the High Court, statutory interpretation, and the doctrine of precedent.
